Ahead of the Tape
Ahead of the Tape
Ahead of the Tape
By Ann Davis
558 words
31 March 2008
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2008 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Quick Recoveries
May Not Be
What You Think
As "corrections" go, this one was quick.
A week ago, many skeptics thought they saw the commodities bubble bursting. Now, despite small declines
Friday, oil is less than $5 shy of its $110.33-a-barrel exchange record, gold again has been bouncing closer
to $1,000 an ounce, and corn has returned to a lofty $5.61 a bushel.
To be fair, a mini-bubble of sorts did burst. The onslaught of investors who this year parked money in hard
assets as a hedge against rising inflation -- and who couldn't find good parking spots in stocks or bonds --
turned and ran from commodities. One trigger: March 19 the Federal Reserve issued a hawkish statement on
inflation, leaving some investors to think it might not let commodity prices keep soaring.
These newcomers had been among the troublemakers behind oil's puzzlingly quick march from $100 a barrel
to $110 amid an ailing economy. Economist Philip Verleger estimates $6 billion flooded into investments that
track commodity indexes following the Fed interest-rate cut of Jan. 22; he then identifies a drop around March
18.
Banks and exchanges have been raising margin requirements as futures markets grow more volatile.
Investors have had to set aside more cash just to keep up their bets, and that has forced some to unwind big
positions. Mr. Verleger points out that the 1979-80 silver bubble "was broken by the raising of margin
requirements."
But many others who allocated a small chunk of their assets to commodities much earlier than that -- ranging
from pension funds to little guys -- aren't budging. From the look of prices, recent lows of $100 for oil and
$918 for gold are now called "buying opportunities."
The report Monday from the Agriculture Department on how much land U.S. farmers will devote to certain
crops could renew the buying frenzy in grain futures. Already, corn stocks at the end of this year's harvest are
projected to hit record lows, and some analysts see less corn planted this season than last year.
But if there isn't a bubble in commodities yet, quick recoveries like the past week's could easily lead to one.
Retrenchments that don't last can contribute to bubble mentalities. Crude fell $10 over just six trading days
but swiftly reversed course to rise 4.7% from the March 24 low.
As in the frothy tech-stock market of 1999 and 2000, investors might be getting used to pullbacks that are
mere resting points before the gallop resumes. Energy analyst Stephen Schork ranted Wednesday that oil's
rise proves "once again that markets can remain illogical far longer than you or we can remain solvent."
Citigroup analyst Tobias Levkovich warned a day earlier of the danger of ignoring historical trends. "It is
critical to realize that commodity prices do go down during recessions and have done so for almost 40 years."
All the talk of a commodity "super cycle" wrongly implies, he says, that this time the rules don't apply.
But Wall Street firm Goldman Sachs, a staunch commodity bull, says those that view this retrenchment,
whatever is left of it, as a buying opportunity will have the last laugh.

Undertow of Worry Again Tugs on Stocks --- After an Initial Climb, Shares End Lower; DJIA Falls 1.2% in Week
Friday's Markets
Undertow of Worry Again Tugs on Stocks --- After an Initial Climb, Shares End Lower; DJIA Falls 1.2%
in Week
By Mark Gongloff
498 words
29 March 2008
The Wall Street Journal
J
B1
English
Copyright 2008 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
The stock market tried but failed to shake off its worries Friday, tying a grim ribbon on a mostly downbeat
week.
Stocks climbed to start the day, but anxiety lurked beneath the cheer and eventually resurfaced, dragging the
Dow Jones Industrial Average down 86.06 points to 12216.40, its third straight decline. It lost 1.2% for the
week and the blue-chip average is down 14% from its October record and 7.9% for the year.
The Standard & Poor's 500-stock index fell 10.54 to 1315.22. It dropped 1.1% on the week and is down 10%
this year. The Nasdaq Composite Index declined 19.65 to 2261.18, off 15% for the year.
For each bit of good news Friday, there was bad news to offset it.
Citigroup upgraded investment bank Lehman Brothers Holdings, whose shares have fallen sharply amid talk
of liquidity troubles. But options traders were still betting heavily against Lehman and another broker that has
suffered during the credit crunch, Merrill Lynch. Lehman ended the day down 2.2% at $37.87, while Merrill
lost 4.7% to 39.93, pulling other financials lower. Citi and American Express were the worst performers in the
Dow industrials, falling 4.4% to 20.83 and 3.8% to 43.15, respectively.
Also weighing on financials was the fate of mortgage lender Fremont General. The Federal Deposit Insurance
Corp. ordered Fremont, once a major subprime lender, to raise capital or sell its banking unit. Fremont shares
fell 15% to 52 cents. Government-backed mortgage lenders Fannie Mae and Freddie Mac dropped 7% to
26.02 and 6% to 25.45, respectively.
A Commerce Department report on personal income and spending wasn't entirely abysmal -- high praise for
an economic report these days -- raising hopes the economy might yet avoid a deep recession.
But J.C. Penney cut its earnings forecast, citing poor sales. That aggravated concerns about the strength of
consumer spending, and the retailer's shares fell 7.5% to 37.48. The Dow Jones Wilshire U.S. retailing-stocks
index fell 1.9%.
"There was a real tug of war between a couple of positives and a couple of negatives," said Todd Clark,
director of trading at Nollenberger Capital Partners in San Francisco.
So it has gone for months. Time and again, stocks have been pummeled by worries about the health of the
credit markets and economy, only to be snatched higher, typically by some dramatic Federal Reserve action.
Stocks rallied in the week ended March 20, after a Fed rate cut and creative liquidity measures. But they
retreated for the past week, albeit on fairly light trading volume.
OIL: Oil futures fell $1.96, or 1.8%, to $105.62 a barrel.
GOLD: Gold prices fell $18.20, or 2%, to $930.60 a troy ounce.

Prudent Bear's Approach Delivers Payoff --- Tice's Pessimism Leads to Gains, But No Second Date
Fund Track
Prudent Bear's Approach Delivers Payoff --- Tice's Pessimism Leads to Gains, But No Second Date
By Reshma Kapadia SmartMoney
860 words
27 March 2008
The Wall Street Journal
J
C2
English
Copyright 2008 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Sometimes, a grim view of the economy can cramp your style. During a recent first date, says David Tice,
manager of Prudent Bear Fund, he made the mistake of sharing his bleak investment outlook at dinner. No
second date followed. "You can almost feel the energy go from the room," Mr. Tice said about when he offers
his take on the economy.
But as an investor, Mr. Tice is on a roll. By selling short and maintaining a relentlessly downbeat look at the
market, his $1.2 billion mutual fund is up more than 15% in the past year, almost 20 percentage points ahead
of the market. His bad-news-bear approach is just getting started: He argues the U.S. is entering a multiyear
bear cycle.
His views haven't always been winners. During its first three years, Prudent Bear lost almost 19% on average
annually from 1996 to 1999, according to Morningstar, when the bull market was steamrolling bears like him.
"We never said we would always make money," said Mr. Tice, who adds that his fund isn't intended to be
anyone's core holding.
Question: What needs to happen to get us back on track?
Mr. Tice: We're going to have to take some pain. We'll have to reconfigure our economy less towards
consumption and more towards manufacturing more products that can be sold abroad. Our standard of living
is going to have to come down.
Question: What's the worst-case scenario?
Mr. Tice: The Federal Reserve's policy to always bail out the economy was a big mistake. The dollar is a
huge problem because foreigners see us continuing to debase our currency. It's going to be a massive
recession if not a depression. The fixes don't really fix anything; they just make it worse.
Question: Since 1982, we've been in a long-term bull market, which means your bearish view hasn't panned
out. What do you say to skeptics?
Mr. Tice: We essentially missed the extent to which policy makers would go to keep the economy going and
how much credit would grow, especially to the lower-end borrower.
Question: What do you make of the comparisons between the U.S. and Japan's lost decade in the 1990s?
Mr. Tice: In 1989, the Nikkei was at 35000. Now it is [below] 13000. They had a humongous real-estate
bubble there, but they were different in that they loaned money to themselves. We have depended on foreign
creditors, so I think it could be worse.
Question: So what are you shorting these days?
Mr. Tice: We're widely diversified. We don't take more than a 1.2% position in any stock and are investing in
larger companies to dampen volatility. We're playing in consumer-discretionary names, banks/brokerage
firms and technology names. Last year, tech was represented as a safe-haven area away from carnage of
financials, but there is a huge consumer sensitivity to lot of technology.
Question: Are we talking about the Apple and Googles of the world?
Mr. Tice: I don't want to talk names, but chip companies are also dependent on consumer demand.
Question: What about retailers?

--- Page 9 ---
Page 9 of 212 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Mr. Tice: We moved away from some of these names in January because so many hedge funds were short
those stocks, and it got crowded. We're also not in housing but more housing suppliers.
Question: What stocks do you own?
Mr. Tice: We are still long precious-metals stocks -- gold, silver, uranium. We like several Canadian
companies -- like Silverstone Resources -- with market caps between $50 million to $5 billion. We're trying to
identify companies that are still under-recognized and have operating leverage. We are also invested in
copper company Capstone Mining, which we have seen a 10-fold profit in but still like.
Question: As a contrarian, does it trouble you that everyone likes gold, too?
Mr. Tice: A little bit. We have less invested than two years ago. Over five years, gold has risen a lot.
However, we still think it has a long way to run. The Dow Jones [Industrial Average]-to-gold price ratio has
ranged from 43 to 3, and right now it's near 15. I think it will go to single digits.
Question: Where do you see other bubbles building?
Mr. Tice: China, emerging markets, the spread between the two-year bond and 10-year, the art market.
Question: What would have to happen for you to become bullish on the U.S. market/economy?
Mr. Tice: I want to get bullish again, but I think these excesses and imbalances are going to take a while to
play out. This is probably a once-in-a-100-year cycle. We will have to have a 40%-plus decline in the U.S.
stock market, plus a massive adjustment to the U.S. economy, away from consumption towards saving and
investment, resulting in massive closures of various retailers and a dramatically reduced current-account
deficit.

Dow Slips a Bit as Investors Show Faith After Dismal News
Tuesday's Markets
Dow Slips a Bit as Investors Show Faith After Dismal News
By Peter A. McKay
529 words
26 March 2008
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2008 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Bleak news about home prices and consumer confidence did little to hurt the stock market, a sign that recent
gains could have staying power, or at least that investors have grown a bit less skittish.
After rising more than 400 points in two days, the Dow Jones Industrial Average spent much of Tuesday
slightly in the red, finishing down 16.04 points, or 0.1%, at 12532.60. Other indexes rose a bit.
The Dow is now down 5.5% this year and 12% below its record close in October.
Analysts said financial markets are continuing to wrestle with the implications of recent moves by the Fed
aimed at establishing a framework for more stable credit trading and a rebound in economic growth, including
interest-rate cuts and its backing of the acquisition of Bear Stearns by J.P. Morgan Chase. by
"The Fed still has a lot of work to do," said Kathy Lien, chief strategist at DailyFX.com, a New York
currency-research firm. She expects payroll data due out April 4 to further spook investors about the
prospects for a broader U.S. economic recovery.
The dollar fell and commodity prices jumped, reviving a trend many investors hoped was over.
The Standard & Poor's 500-stock index inched up 0.2%, or 3.11 points, to 1352.99. It was led by its
basic-materials sector, up 2.7% on the heels of the rally in raw materials. The S&P is down 7.9% this year
and off 14% from its record in October.
The technology-stock-heavy Nasdaq Composite Index rose 0.6%, or 14.30, to 2341.05, though it remains
down 12% in 2008.
David James, senior vice president of James Investment Research in Alpha, Ohio, said his firm has been
advising clients to bet more aggressively on stocks since shortly after the Fed's March 11 decision to accept
a wider range of credit securities -- including many distressed issues -- as collateral for its loans to financial
firms.
But Mr. James also believes the stock market's rally will be short-term, lasting perhaps two or three months
amid a bear market and anemic economic growth that could last several years. "With a credit problem like we
have now, it gets right to the heart of what drives businesses and consumer activity," he said. "Once you've
entered a problem like this, it can take a while to solve."
Economic news Tuesday offered investors little to cheer about. The Standard & Poor's/Case-Shiller index
showed home prices in 10 U.S. cities slid 11.4% in January, the steepest drop since the index began in 1987.
The Conference Board issued a worse-than-expected reading of consumer confidence for March.
Raw materials, down sharply last week, benefited from a return of speculative buyers. A weakening dollar
also helped commodities, which are priced globally in dollars.
The broad-based Dow Jones-AIG Commodity Index gained 1.9%, or 3.785 points, to finish at 203.301. Gold
and oil futures rose, snapping a three-day losing streak.

Page 11 of 212 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Stocks Tarnished By 'Lost Decade' --- U.S. Shares in Longest Funk Since 1970s; Credit Crunch Could Prolong Weakness
Stocks Tarnished By 'Lost Decade' --- U.S. Shares in Longest Funk Since 1970s; Credit Crunch Could
Prolong Weakness
By E.S. Browning
2,093 words
26 March 2008
The Wall Street Journal
J
A1
English
Copyright 2008 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Over the past 200 years, the stock market's steady upward march occasionally has been disrupted for long
stretches, most recently during the Great Depression and the inflation-plagued 1970s. The current market
turmoil suggests that we may be in another lost decade.
The stock market is trading right where it was nine years ago. Stocks, long touted as the best investment for
the long term, have been one of the worst investments over the nine-year period, trounced even by lowly
Treasury bonds.
The Standard & Poor's 500-stock index, the basis for about half of the $1 trillion invested in U.S. index funds,
finished at 1352.99 on Tuesday, below the 1362.80 it hit in April 1999. When dividends and inflation are
factored into returns, the S&P 500 has risen an average of just 1.3% a year over the past 10 years, well
below the historical norm, according to Morningstar Inc. For the past nine years, it has fallen 0.37% a year,
and for the past eight, it is off 1.4% a year. In light of the current wobbly market, some economists and market
analysts worry that the era of disappointing returns may not be over.
Until last fall, many investors had viewed the bursting of the tech-stock bubble as a nasty but short-term
setback. The market had resumed its upward march, reaching new highs in October. Then the credit crisis
began weighing on stocks, as did the possibility of a recession. By March 10, the S&P 500 was down 18.6%
from its Oct. 9 record close, nearing the 20% decline that signals a bear market. It has rebounded since then
amid the Federal Reserve's efforts to stabilize the financial system, but it remains 13.3% below its October
record.
Conventional stock-market wisdom holds that if investors buy a broad range of stocks and hold them, they
will do better than they would in other investments. But that rule hasn't held up for stocks bought in the late
1990s or 2000.
Over the past nine years, the S&P 500 is the worst-performing of nine different investment vehicles tracked
by Morningstar, including commodities, real-estate investment trusts, gold and foreign stocks. Big U.S.
stocks were outrun even by Treasury bonds, which historically perform much less well than stocks. Adjusted
for inflation, Treasurys are up 4.7% a year over the past nine years, and up 5.8% a year since the March
2000 stock peak. An index of commodities has shown about twice the annual gains of bonds, as have
real-estate investment trusts.
Stocks also underperformed other investments during the 1930s and the 1970s. During both of those periods,
stocks would rally strongly, only to fade. It took well over a decade in each case for stocks to move lastingly
upward.
So far, the current decade hasn't featured the high inflation of the 1970s or the high unemployment of the
1930s. That makes some analysts and economists hopeful that the stock troubles won't be as bad or last as
long as they did back then, despite the housing crisis and the breakdown in parts of the mortgage and lending
businesses. Many of them hope that the Federal Reserve will do a better job of righting the ship than it did in
those prior decades.
Finance professor Jeremy Siegel at the University of Pennsylvania's Wharton School has written about stock
behavior back into the 19th century. During the past decade, he points out, the worst years were from 2000
through 2002, when stocks fell sharply. Although the S&P 500 has been inconsistent since then -- rising
strongly in 2003, then registering single-digit gains in 2004, 2005 and 2007 -- he considers the bad times
largely past. Other optimists agree.

--- Page 12 ---
Page 12 of 212 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
But Yale economist Robert Shiller, who predicted the market trouble in his 2000 book "Irrational Exuberance,"
warns that the market still hasn't shaken off its excesses. He and some other analysts think the latest volatility
is a symptom of more trouble to come.
"I have to say that this isn't a great time to be in the stock market," says Prof. Shiller. "The housing crisis that
we are going through is going to put a damper on the economy that is longer than a recession. I don't see the
stock troubles ending as quickly as many people are imagining."
Historically, stocks rise about two years out of every three, for an average gain of 7% a year when controlled
for inflation, according to Prof. Siegel. Stocks have shown gains for almost every 10-year period since 1925
-- 98.6% of the time, according to Ned Davis Research.
But when stock investing becomes a mania, as it did in the 1920s, the 1960s and the 1990s, it leads to
prolonged periods of subpar performance, according to financial historian Richard Sylla of New York
University's Stern School of Business.
Prof. Sylla has examined stock booms and busts back to 1800. He found periods of exceptional strength in
the late 1810s and early 1820s, the 1840s, the 1860s and the early 1900s. Those periods were followed by
lengthy weakness in the 1830s, the 1850s, the 1870s and before 1920. In a 2001 paper, he forecast a
10-year period of stock weakness.
"When you have extraordinary returns, as we did from 1982 through 1999, then usually the next 10 years
aren't very good," says Prof. Sylla. His research suggests that exceptional booms steal gains from the future.
When the booms end, returns become subpar, so that average returns over the longer term fall back to the
7% norm. Economists call this "reversion to the mean," the idea that exceptional performance can't last
forever.
Bullish investors believed that the bad days were over late in 2002, when stocks rebounded following the
technology-stock wreck, the Sept. 11 terrorist attacks and the collapse of Enron Corp.
The S&P 500 rose 26% in 2003, amid hopes for a quick victory in Iraq. In 2004, the S&P 500 rose only 9%. It
was up 3% in 2005, 14% in 2006 and 3.5% in 2007. The index is down 7.9% so far this year. Those numbers
are not adjusted for inflation, which would lower annual returns by a few percentage points.
The Dow Jones Industrial Average, which had fewer technology stocks than the S&P 500 and suffered less in
the bear market from 2000 to 2002, has held up better, but not a lot better. It has risen less than 1% a year
since January 2000.
Prof. Sylla expects to see stocks turn more lastingly upward some time in the next two years. The market's
direction will depend partly on the individual investor. The 1990s stock bubble and the bear market that
followed came at a time when more individuals were managing their own retirement savings through 401(k)
accounts, individual retirement accounts and the like.
Individual investors helped create bubbles in the markets for technology stocks and for real estate. In recent
years, investors have been putting far less money into U.S. stocks than they did during the stock-investing
boom. In 2000, at the height of that boom, Americans added $260 billion to U.S.-stock mutual funds,
according to the Investment Company Institute, a trade group. Last year, investors took more money out of
those funds than they put in -- a net outflow of $46.4 billion.
America's shift toward self-managed retirement could soften some of the stock-market volatility. People
appear to be much less likely to move money around in retirement accounts than in other investment
accounts, according to economist John Ameriks at mutual-fund company Vanguard Group.
Many 401(k) participants leave their allocations alone for long periods of time, says Mr. Ameriks. If they set
up their accounts to send money into stocks each month, those accounts tend to keep doing so through bull
and bear markets alike. That may provide some support to stocks.
Some investment advisers say passive contributions like that actually make some sense. People whose
retirement accounts have bought stocks each month, year in and year out, haven't done nearly as badly as
those who bought in the late 1990s and stopped buying, Prof. Sylla says. While the S&P 500 is down since
1999, it is up since mid-2001, meaning that most stock purchased since then by retirement accounts shows a
gain.
A big problem for the market right now is what analysts call stock fundamentals. Strong corporate-profit gains
and low inflation have supported stocks since 2002, but they are becoming harder to sustain.

--- Page 13 ---
Page 13 of 212 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
In a typical year, Prof. Sylla says, corporate profits run at about 5% or 6% of total economic output, after tax.
In 2006, that number was 9%, a record. Historically, this number tends to revert to the mean, suggesting that
profits now could weaken. "Profits may fall to 3% or 4%" of economic output, Prof. Sylla says.
Spending by ordinary people could have an effect on those profits. Consumer borrowing and spending kept
the economy afloat after the stock bubble popped in 2000. Emboldened by high home values, people
borrowed at levels rarely seen, pushing down the national savings rate to zero.
That's what worries Prof. Shiller. After studying the housing market, he sees home values continuing to
weaken for years. He expects consumers to borrow and spend less, and to rebuild their savings.
A consumer pullback would hold back economic growth and corporate profits, putting a damper on U.S. stock
gains and giving investors an incentive to continue putting money into commodities or stocks in Brazil,
Russia, India and China. Baby boomers concerned about retirement income could look for safer investments
with guaranteed returns, such as Treasury bonds and bond-like products offered by mutual-fund companies.
"We have to accept that this is no longer a nation of 4% real economic growth. This is a mature nation that no
longer has a strong manufacturing base," says Steve Leuthold, chairman of Leuthold Weeden Research in
Minneapolis. He believes that another bull market is on the horizon, perhaps following some additional stock
declines. But that future bull market, he contends, could be followed by another bear market that could bring
stocks back close to where they are today.
Before another lengthy bull run can begin, stocks need to overcome two problems: the hangover from the
high prices of the late 1990s, and the continuing effects of the exceptionally low interest rates instituted by
the Federal Reserve in 2001 and again today. Those low interest rates helped push corporate profits higher,
but also fueled borrowing excesses that led to today's economic problems.
To some analysts, stock prices still look inflated. Prof. Shiller calculates that the S&P 500 traded in the late
1990s at more than 40 times its component companies' profits -- far above the historical norm of 16. (To
avoid distortions, he uses average profits over a 10-year period.) Today, the S&P 500 still trades at more than
20 times profits -- still far above average.
"The S&P 500 never got back down to its long-term trend line" after the 1990s, says Jeremy Grantham of
Boston money-management firm Grantham, Mayo, Van Otterloo & Co. Mr. Grantham, who has long warned
of a prolonged period of subpar stock performance, says exceptionally low interest rates temporarily
propped up the indexes.
There are reasons to hope that things won't be as ugly this time as they were either in the 1970s or in the
1990s in Japan, which went into a prolonged slump after bubbles in its housing and stock markets.
For one thing, although inflation has been running above 4% this year, it remains well below the double-digit
rates of the 1970s. That's made it easier for the Fed to stimulate the economy without worrying about
sparking runaway inflation.
One big question is how much worse investor confidence will get. The bearish Mr. Grantham expects
investors to become gloomier, but not as pessimistic as they were during past bad stretches.
"I think the global economy will stay, on balance, not so bad," he says. "There is no reason for people to
become as pessimistic as they did even in Japan, and certainly not as pessimistic as in the Depression."

Financial stocks soared in Europe, Asia and the Americas on hopes the worst of the credit crunch has
passed, giving many indexes their best day in weeks.
In Europe, where most markets reopened Tuesday following a four-day Easter break, the pan-European Dow
Jones Stoxx 600 index jumped 3.3% to 306.63, its highest close since March 13.
Markets in Hong Kong and Mumbai notched gains of more than 6%.
The gains came after a big advance in U.S. stocks Monday, on the back of strong existing-home sales and
J.P. Morgan Chase's increased offer for Bear Stearns. U.S. stocks also jumped Thursday before the market
closed for Good Friday.
Some analysts and investors were skeptical about the staying power of the rallies. The steepest one-month
decline in U.S. consumer confidence in a decade, announced during Tuesday's European trading day, fed
that thinking.
The day's gain made only a small dent in the losses this year of 16% for the Stoxx 600. Most Asian indexes
also are in the red for the year.
"We can have relief rallies for a while and then sell off," said Kostas Iordanidis, co-chief investment officer at
Olympia Capital Management in Paris, which runs funds of hedge funds. "Clearly, we have gone a long way,
but the credit crisis that started in June of last year is not yet over."
One of the day's largest gainers was United Kingdom lender HBOS, which soared 15%. The stock had been
battered for much of the past two weeks, in part on rumors, denied by the bank, that it had sought emergency
funds from the Bank of England. Royal Bank of Scotland Group rose 9.3%. In Zurich, UBS, the European
bank that has disclosed the largest losses related to subprime mortgages, jumped 8.2%.
Technology was the best-performing sector in Europe with a gain of 6.4%. Nokia, which accounts for more
than 40% of the region's technology index, leapt 8.2% in Helsinki after Chief Financial Officer Rick Simonson
told Bloomberg Television that the company hadn't felt much of an impact from the U.S. downturn.
In LONDON, the FTSE 100 rose 3.5% to 5689.10, its largest one-day percentage gain since Jan. 24. Taylor
Wimpey, a U.K. home builder with operations in the U.S., surged 12%. British Energy rose 6.4% on a
weekend report that Centrica, which operates in North America, the U.K. and Western Europe, is weighing a
GBP 10 billion bid ($19.85 billion) for the nuclear-power generator. Centrica rose 1.7%.
In FRANKFURT, the German DAX 30 rose 3.2% to 6524.71. Adidas rose 6.1% on a deal that allows Brazil's
Vulcabras to distribute Reebok apparel and footwear in Brazil and Paraguay. The venture should lead
Reebok sales to increase, at constant currencies, at a mid-to-high single-digit-percentage rate. Adidas
previously forecast Reebok's top-line growth in a low-to-mid single-digit range.
Alitalia skyrocketed 33% in Milan on hopes for an improved offer from Air France-KLM.Air France-KLM ended
up 0.2% in Paris.
In HONG KONG, the Hang Seng Index, which also reopened after a four-day Easter weekend, jumped 6.4%,
its largest one-day gain since Jan. 25, to 22464.52. Financials fronted the recovery. HSBC Holdings gained
5.2%, while Bank of East Asia surged 9.4%.

steelmakers, taking the Bovespa to its third consecutive high. Petrobras shares rose 5.3% on rising oil prices.

Grain and Oilseed Post Gains on Weakness in Dollar
Commodities Report
Grain and Oilseed Post Gains on Weakness in Dollar
By Andrew Johnson Jr.
565 words
26 March 2008
The Wall Street Journal
J
C5
English
Copyright 2008 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Weakness in the dollar served as the catalyst for a rise in Chicago Board of Trade grain and oilseed futures.
Wheat rose nearly 5%, while soybeans and corn posted gains of nearly 4% on the day.
CBOT May wheat closed up 47.5 cents to $10.6750 a bushel. May soybeans settled up the
exchange-imposed 50-cent limit at $13.07, and May corn finished up the 20-cent limit at $5.4475 a bushel.
Weakness in the U.S. dollar and climbing gold prices generated bullish momentum in the grain complex, said
Dale Durchholz, analyst with Agrivisor in Bloomington, Ill.
Grain markets slumped last week as jitters about the economy weakened financial markets and sparked fears
that grains could slip into a downtrend.
Now, "the economic situation on the outside looks a little more stable and more money is flowing into the
market," said Shawn McCambridge, analyst for Prudential Bache in Chicago.
"It's kind of a Dr. Jekyll and Mr. Hyde kind of attitude," the analyst said. Tuesday's up move "is just kind of a
reverse mirror image of what was going on last week."
Soybean prices were also buoyed by a lingering farmers' strike in Argentina, as traders wonder if the situation
will cause global soybean buyers to shift purchases from Argentina to the U.S. or other suppliers.
Analysts also expect soybean futures to maintain price strength ahead of the U.S. Agriculture Department's
prospective plantings report, scheduled to be released Monday. The report is the first official government
estimate of what farmers intend to plant for the 2008 crop year.
Acreage also played a role in corn's gains. Corn-planted area in 2008 is expected to be smaller than the 93.6
million acres seeded in 2007, as farmers switch to other crops like wheat and soybeans, even though
demand for corn isn't expected to decline.
While wheat futures posted the largest percentage gain on the day, the gains were largely in response to the
weaker U.S. currency and the limit up moves in corn and soybeans. Wheat futures are expected to take
further direction from the prospective-plantings report as well as a quarterly grain-stocks report that is also
scheduled to be released Monday.
In other commodity markets:
CRUDE OIL: Futures crept higher to close above $101 a barrel in a market torn between economic worries
and a weaker dollar. Light, sweet crude for May delivery settled up 36 cents, or 0.4%, at $101.22 a barrel on
the New York Mercantile Exchange, snapping a three-day losing streak.
Crude is now off 8.3% from its Nymex record close of $110.33 hit on March 13 but is still up 61% from 52
weeks ago. Buying was underpinned by the dollar's weakening against rival currencies, which has historically
drawn speculators seeking a hedge against the currency's slide and inflation.
A propane leak at BP PLC's Texas City, Texas, refinery helped stoke supply jitters in the market, also
keeping buying sentiment high.
GOLD: Prices on the Comex division of the Nymex rose as investors viewed gold as a good hedge against
the decline in the U.S. dollar. March gold gained $16.30 to $934.60 a troy ounce.

Tom Polansek contributed to this article.

3.6%.

Moving The Market / Currency Trading -- Forex View: Fed's Restraint May Lead Dollar To Continue Rise
Moving The Market / Currency Trading -- Forex View: Fed's Restraint May Lead Dollar To Continue
Rise
By Dan Molinski
634 words
24 March 2008
The Wall Street Journal
J
C2
English
Copyright 2008 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Slumping commodity prices and a Federal Reserve that has begun to exhibit restraint in cutting interest
rates may allow the dollar's modest rally to continue this week.
Investors believe the dollar may have finally found a champion in the Fed after the U.S. central bank
reduced interest rates less than expected last week and noted that it is more worried than before about
inflation. That could pave the way to an eventual end to the rate-reduction cycle, which could attract
investors back into the greenback.
"We've seen a moderate comeback in the dollar," said John Shin, currency strategist at Lehman Brothers in
New York. "The Fed's decision is, to some extent, consistent with the idea that policy makers don't want to
see a dollar in free fall."
Commodity prices might be the key to the dollar's performance this week. Oil prices fell at their sharpest rate
in years last week, and if investors believe a longer-term trend toward price declines is afoot, the dollar
stands to gain. Hedge funds that used dollars to buy commodities may continue to unwind those bets, parking
their funds back in greenbacks.
With all of this as a backdrop, analysts are forecasting the euro could trade between $1.530-$1.560 this
week, while the dollar is likely to drift between 98 Japanese yen and 101 yen.
The euro was at $1.5439 late Friday in light global trading, compared with $1.5417 the day earlier, while the
dollar was at 99.42 yen, up from 99.19 yen. The U.K. pound was at $1.9825, compared with $1.9838, and the
dollar was at 1.0089 Swiss francs, from 1.0115 francs.
Some of the dollar's swing higher last week came as a result of its appealing status as the comeback kid of
currencies, as the recent sharp declines have left the greenback oversold against many rivals. That support
could erode fast if U.S. housing data due this week come in as poor as expected and remind investors why
the U.S. economy and the dollar are in the dumps, or if gold and oil prices rebound.
"We interpret the dollar rally of the past days as a natural phase of consolidation after such a sharp decline,"
said Bilal Hafeez, currency strategist at Deutsche Bank. "We keep a core long-euro-versus-dollar position,
looking for new highs [in the euro] once we pass this phase of consolidation."
Still, some analysts think the dollar may have seen the last of its nose dives that caused some to suggest
major central banks would have to intervene in markets to halt the U.S. currency's slide. Also, investors are
hoping the credit crunch could be easing thanks to the Fed's massive liquidity-pumping actions and
government plans to help struggling home owners.
If such sentiment continues this week, look for currency investors to refocus on economics, both in the U.S.
and globally. This could be good for the dollar if more indicators suggest the euro-zone economy is starting to
feel the effects of the downswing in the U.S.
Against the yen, the dollar may face a more-difficult time this week, as the Japanese currency has also
gained broadly on the back of lower commodity prices. Short-term investors who had bet big on
commodity-linked currencies such as Australian and Canadian dollars did so with borrowed yen to take
advantage of the low interest rates offered in Japan. As those investors sell the Australian and Canadian
dollars, some are buying yen to pay back those loans from Japan, providing support to the Japanese
currency.

Commodity Break
REVIEW & OUTLOOK (Editorial)
Commodity Break
293 words
24 March 2008
The Wall Street Journal
J
A14
English
Copyright 2008 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Amid last week's market tumult, there was one glimmer of good news: The first break in weeks in the
commodity price bubble that had been building since the Federal Reserve signaled that price stability was
well down its list of priorities.
On Tuesday, two members of the Fed's Open Market Committee formally dissented from the decision to cut
the fed funds rate by another 75 basis points, to 2.25%. Markets had expected a cut of 100 points. Even that
new and mild Fed caution about inflation seems to have taken the edge off speculators who had been
betting that the dollar would continue falling to new lows amid U.S. malign neglect.
In the wake of that dissent, gold took a deep dive, falling to $910 an ounce from above $1,000. Oil also
headed down -- to below $102 a barrel from nearly $110 -- and other commodities fell from speculative highs.
All of this is good news for consumers, who have watched this commodity boom lift energy and fuel prices
and reduce their discretionary spending power.
This is further evidence that the commodity price boom is rooted in the Fed's weak dollar policy, and not in a
change in "relative prices" due to rising global demand. Commodity prices have soared since September in
particular, even as the world economy has slowed down. The "global demand" argument has become a Wall
Street-Washington excuse for easier money in the mistaken belief that the Fed can inflate away U.S. credit
problems. Instead, the commodity boom has hurt consumer and business confidence precisely when they are
most needed. The Fed still has a long way to go to restore its credibility, but last week was at least a start.

An ETN that shorts gold by doubling down on futures prices rose 21% in just the three days that ended
ahead of the Good Friday shutdown for U.S. markets, as the metal and other commodities continued to
plunge following the Federal Reserve's rate cut and inflation warnings.
Until last week, gold had been soaring, to more than $1,000 a troy ounce. And, in fact, gold bulls insist this
downturn is only a pause in a longer-term rally.
That may be so, but you wouldn't have known it by last week's action, when DB Gold Double Short ETN,
which is issued by Deutsche Bank, gained roughly 7% on each of the three days preceding Friday -- amid a
broad selloff in hard assets such as precious metals, oil and wheat. Commodity prices corrected hard after a
prolonged rally driven by Fed interest-rate cuts, a weak dollar and recession fears heightened by the credit
crisis.
Gold prices saw their steepest decline in nearly two years Wednesday after the Fed said uncertainty about
the outlook for inflation has increased. A rising dollar also makes dollar-denominated commodities such as
gold more expensive for foreign investors.
There is speculation that leveraged investors are selling profitable commodities positions to raise cash to
offset losses elsewhere.
"A strategy designed to use base commodities to protect investors from inflation, at a time when economic
growth is slowing and underlying demand for commodities is weakening, is unsustainable," said Thorsten
Fischer, an economist at the Royal Bank of Scotland.
Designed to provide leveraged, inverse exposure to already volatile gold prices, the ETN's ticker symbol,
DZZ, could be appropriate given its potential to make investors dizzy with big gains or losses in short periods.
In late February, Deutsche Bank listed a trio of gold ETNs, which are similar to exchange-traded funds, or
ETFs, on the NYSE Arca electronic arm. The other two are DB Gold Double Long ETN (symbol: DGP) and
DB Gold Short ETN (DGZ). All three have annual expenses of 0.75%.
One of the latest trends in ETFs and ETNs is products that give individual investors the ability to take
leveraged or short positions in various sectors.
For example, DB Gold Double Short ETN provides twice the monthly inverse, or opposite, return of gold
futures. In addition to the return from gold's price movement, investors earn a yield based on an index of
three-month U.S. Treasury bills.
Therefore, if gold prices fell 6% during a month, the ETN would aim to deliver a 12% positive return, not
including the Treasury yield, although the actual results may not line up exactly, as leverage changes over
time based on the movement of gold prices.
Conversely, if gold rose, the ETN investors would stand to lose money because it provides short exposure to
the metal.
DB Gold Short ETN provides unleveraged short exposure. So if gold slipped 6% for the month, the ETN is
programmed to gain 6%. The other product, DB Gold Double Long ETN, gives straight leveraged exposure to
gold prices.

--- Page 26 ---
Page 26 of 212 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
"Gold has had quite a run-up, so the correction was not entirely unexpected," said Kevin Rich, managing
director in Deutsche Bank's global-markets investment-products group.
A massive ETF that holds actual gold bars, StreetTracks Gold Shares (GLD), also saw heavy trading last
week. It lost ground as the short-side ETNs gained, but it remains up more than 9% for the year to date and
has doubled over a three-year period.
The Deutsche Bankgold ETNs are different from leveraged and inverse ETFs from ProShares and Rydex
Investments that seek to give double or the opposite of the daily return of an index, minus expenses. In other
words, the leverage is reset on a daily basis to produce the desired effect.
In contrast, Deutsche Bank's gold ETNs perform this task on a monthly basis, in an effort to leverage or short
the metal's longer-term performance. During the month, the leverage levels on DB Gold Double Long ETN
and DB Gold Double Short ETN can move away from 2-to-1, especially in volatile markets. Still, they should
deliver close to the desired leverage on a monthly basis.
ProShares is reportedly readying ETFs that would give leveraged and inverse exposure to commodity
indexes including gold, so the Deutsche Bank ETNs could see competition.

Politics & Economics: Paulson Takes Lead Amid Crisis
Politics & Economics: Paulson Takes Lead Amid Crisis
By Michael M. Phillips, Damian Paletta and Greg Ip
992 words
22 March 2008
The Wall Street Journal
J
A4
English
Copyright 2008 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
WASHINGTON -- Last Sunday, during a frantic weekend in which the housing crisis pushed a venerable Wall
Street firm toward bankruptcy, Treasury Secretary Henry Paulson decided it was time to twist arms at
mortgage giants Fannie Mae and Freddie Mac.
On a conference call with the chief executives of both lenders, Mr. Paulson urged Fannie and Freddie --
private companies with implicit government backing -- to raise $10 billion each in new capital and boost the
number of mortgages they could finance. His message: The foreclosure crisis and the credit crunch it
spawned are now too serious for half-steps and hesitation. Freddie Mac's Richard Syron and Fannie Mae's
Daniel Mudd, neither of whom had been eager to go fund-raising just a few days before, agreed to seek
billions in new capital to help revive the shocked mortgage market.
"We've got an opportunity to . . . do a good thing for the markets," Mr. Paulson told the housing executives,
according to a person on the call.
This past week, the federal government suddenly became a big factor in the search for a solution to the
housing crisis after months of incremental measures. The Fannie/Freddie move was one of a series from the
Treasury, the Federal Reserve and other U.S. authorities to stabilize markets and reassure homeowners, as
many as two million of whom are expected to enter foreclosure this year.
There is no telling whether this will prove a pivotal point in the economic and financial downturn that began
last summer. But a week that began with disaster did settle down. The Dow Jones Industrial Average rose
3.4% through Thursday to 12361.32 -- its best performance since the week ending Feb. 1. Oil, copper, gold
and silver prices fell, while the dollar climbed 1.6% to $1.5417 per euro. U.S. markets were closed Friday.
"Everyone was expecting this to be a terrible week, and instead it was one of the better weeks we've had in a
long time," said Jason Furman, a Brookings Institution senior fellow. But, he added: "We're very far from out
of the woods."
The government's actions this past week marked a noted uptick in federal activism. They also solidified Mr.
Paulson's position within the administration as the crisis's point man. Previously, he had drawn criticism from
Democrats and some in the financial industry for an approach centering on voluntary moves by the mortgage
industry to modify loan terms for troubled borrowers.
Friday, Senate Banking Committee Chairman Christopher Dodd (D., Conn.) called on Alphonso Jackson,
secretary of Housing and Urban Development, to resign. He questioned Mr. Jackson's leadership and the
agency's operations during the housing crisis. Mr. Jackson, through a spokeswoman, said he plans to remain
in office, and the White House expressed its support.
How much further the administration will go is unclear. The White House has publicly remained adamant that
it won't support a bailout for the housing market. House Financial Services Committee Chairman Barney
Frank (D., Mass.) is pushing a bill that would provide hundreds of billions of dollars in federal guarantees to
let hard-hit homeowners refinance their mortgages.
"You've got a tension here," White House economic adviser Edward Lazear said in an interview Friday. "You
want to make sure that you keep people in their homes to the extent possible, but you don't want to reward
speculators. You don't want to reward unscrupulous lenders and so forth."
Treasury officials contacted Mr. Frank's office this week to discuss his bill, a sign Democrats took as evidence
the administration is willing to compromise.
Three days after the conference call with Mr. Paulson, Messrs. Mudd and Syron stood beside their top
regulator -- Office of Federal Housing Enterprise Oversight Director James Lockhart -- and announced they

--- Page 28 ---
Page 28 of 212 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
would raise "significant" capital. Mr. Lockhart, in turn, agreed to remove some restrictions on the companies,
permitting them to buy an additional $200 billion in mortgages and mortgage-backed securities.
Treasury officials also privately encouraged the Federal Housing Finance Board to allow the nation's 12
Federal Home Loan Banks to buy as much as $160 billion in mortgage-backed securities, creating even more
liquidity for markets. The finance board, a little-known government agency, is expected to approve the
measure Monday.
Together, the moves by Ofheo and the finance board will let government-chartered firms buy as much as
$360 billion in mortgage-related assets, a potentially huge injection of liquidity into a dry market.
At the same time, the Federal Reserve stepped into uncharted territory. Two weeks ago, the Fed began
devising a way to support the mortgage-backed securities market, which was left in limbo by defaults by
homeowners. Tuesday, the Fed unveiled a plan to lend as much as $200 billion of its own much-sought
Treasurys to bond dealers for up to 28 days. In return, it would take temporary ownership of their
now-shunned mortgage-backed securities.
It proved only a short-term help. Lenders and counterparties already had begun refusing to do business with
Bear Stearns Cos. Friday morning, the Fed made a 28-day loan to the firm via J.P. Morgan Chase & Co.,
using its emergency power to lend to nonbanks from its discount window for the first time since the Great
Depression. But lender skittishness grew that day, threatening to pull in more investment banks.
The Fed followed Tuesday with a 0.75 percentage point cut in short-term rates to 2.25%, bringing total
trimming since September to three percentage points, its most rapid pace of easing in decades.
"The Fed has gained some important 'street cred' over the past two weeks with its latest salvo of rate cuts
and credit facilities designed to stave off financial and economic Armageddon," Scott Anderson, Wells Fargo
senior economist, wrote to clients Friday.

Gold's Drop Doesn't Faze Bulls --- Inflation Fears Suggest Commodities Correction Will Be Short-Lived
Commodities Report
Gold's Drop Doesn't Faze Bulls --- Inflation Fears Suggest Commodities Correction Will Be
Short-Lived
By Matt Whittaker and Allen Sykora
773 words
22 March 2008
The Wall Street Journal
J
A14
English
Copyright 2008 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
A roughly 10% drop in gold prices from record highs in the past week doesn't portend a longer and deeper
correction for the metal, because inflation concerns are likely to continue, and sellers will probably return as
buyers into what has been a bullish market.
"We've needed a healthy correction in commodities," says Bill O'Neill, a principal with Logic Advisors. "This
may be a signal that the corrections may not be lasting in nature. This does not signal the end of
commodities."
The nearby-month March gold contract has fallen nearly 10% from an all-time front-month high of $1,014.60
an ounce on Monday to Thursday's low of $917.70. It settled down $25.10 to $919.60 on the Comex division
of the New York Mercantile Exchange, ahead of Good Friday, when markets were closed.
Over that same period, most-active April gold futures have fallen more than 12% from a contract high of
$1,033.90 on Monday to Thursday's low of $904.70. April gold declined $25.30 to settle at $920.
"Longer term, I still think gold is pretty much a decent bet," said Bart Melek, global commodity strategist with
BMO Capital Markets.
The perception that inflation may increase on "very accommodating monetary policy" from the Federal
Reserve will likely keep commodities in an upward direction, Mr. Melek said.
"I do expect the markets to come back," said Frank Lesh, broker and futures analyst with Future Path
Trading. "A lot of it just depends on the outlook for the economy and interest rates. We don't have a clear
picture of things right now."
Hedge-fund-led selling on Wednesday and Thursday was responsible for much of the losses, as the dollar
rose after a smaller-than-expected interest-rate cut from the Federal Reserve on Tuesday afternoon.
March gold on Wednesday saw the biggest drop in dollar terms in more than 28 years -- since Jan. 22, 1980,
when it fell $143.50. Wednesday's was the largest percentage decline since July 13, 2006.
However, the past week's slide in gold prices doesn't represent a major turnaround for the market, said Scott
Meyers, senior trading analyst with Pioneer Futures.
"I think the trend is still up," he said. "You're seeing a reaction [to the less-than-expected rate cut]."
There are temporary price drivers, for example, such as the funds taking some profits as the end of the first
quarter approaches, Mr. Meyers said.
Daniel Pavilonis, senior market strategist with Lind-Waldock, described himself as "still bullish" on gold. And
in many cases, those who exited from long gold positions are also bullish, he said.
"They've pulled the pin on their positions," he said. "They are holding off, waiting to get back in the market."
Many are waiting for the market to show signs of putting in a bottom before re-entering, and it is possible that
won't occur until the coming week, Mr. Pavilonis said.
Otherwise, inflation remains a concern in the U.S., and investors often buy gold as an inflation hedge, he
said.
"People are still paying more money at the grocery store, and they are paying more money at the pumps," Mr.
Pavilonis said. "As we still have inflation, you're still going to need a safe haven to put your equity."

--- Page 30 ---
Page 30 of 212 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
While the Fed's concern about inflation has boosted the dollar and sent commodities lower, some portion of
that talk may simply be "rhetoric" meant to appease those who see the central bank as not doing enough to
curb rising consumer prices, said Michael Gross, broker and futures analyst with OptionSellers.com.
The week's action in gold isn't likely the beginning of a longer and deeper correction, he said.
"I think this is a knee-jerk reaction to the Fed's announcement," but longer term, the central bank will have to
continue cutting interest rates, which will likely further pressure the dollar and prove supportive for gold, Mr.
Gross said.
"This is the inevitable consolidation that you have to see when markets have run-ups like this," said Logic
Advisors' Mr. O'Neill. "It just came too far too fast."
Mr. O'Neill expressed concern there may be more "shoes to drop" amid continuing troubles in the credit
markets, which have led to safe-haven buying of gold lately, but he said he doesn't foresee the pullback as
the start of a longer-term correction.

Dollar Gains on the Yen In Light Global Trading

The dollar traded in tight ranges with little volume as U.S. stock and bond markets were closed for Good
Friday and no major U.S. economic data were released.
Most European markets were also closed, but in Asian trading Tokyo stocks climbed 1.8%, which fueled
some risk appetite in foreign-exchange markets and weighed on the low-yielding yen. The dollar and the euro
exited active trading slightly higher against the yen Friday.
The dollar barely moved against the euro, suggesting perhaps that the dollar's rally that began this past week
on the back of slumping commodity prices could extend into the coming week.
Still, analysts weren't convinced further dollar gains may be sustained for long given a stagnant U.S.
economy and low interest rates by the Federal Reserve that continue to weigh heavily on the greenback.
"We are not inclined to overinterpret this week's price action," said Nick Bennenbroek, head of currency
strategy at Wells Fargo Bank in New York. "We suspect the dollar will reverse some of its gains in coming
weeks, and suspect a sustained rally in the dollar is some months away."
Friday afternoon in New York, the euro was at $1.5439 from $1.5417 late Thursday, while the dollar was at
99.42 yen from 99.19 yen. The euro was 153.50 yen from 152.92 yen. The U.K. pound was at $1.9825 from
$1.9838 late Thursday, and the dollar was at 1.0089 Swiss francs from 1.0115 francs.
With oil falling back near $100 a barrel this past week and gold down near $920 a troy ounce, many hedge
funds that used dollars to bet heavily on these commodities have shifted back into greenbacks as a safe
haven. That has helped give the dollar its lift during the past few days.
The drop in commodity prices has also fueled a selloff in currencies whose countries are major commodity
producers, such as the Canadian and Australian dollars.
Friday afternoon in New York, the Australian dollar was at $0.9018, near where it closed late Thursday and
well off its $0.9347 high for the week, reached Wednesday.
And the U.S. dollar stood at C$1.0239 Friday afternoon, much higher than its week low of C$0.9879.
Wells Fargo's Mr. Bennenbroek said the Canadian dollar's declines last week may be a sign of more
weakness to come.
"A reversal in oil prices has contributed to the Canadian currency's fall and, given the prospect of further
Canadian rate cuts, we do see the underlying trend for the Canadian dollar as down over a longer time
horizon," he said.
Brazil's currency, the real, also has weakened due to the lower commodity prices. But Brazilian economic
fundamentals remain relatively solid, direct investment inflows are firm and international reserves have been
built up substantially, so the real may see some bounce in the days ahead, Mr. Bennenbroek said.

The Week Ahead / Our Take On Coming Events

Banking:
Chinese Lenders
Set to Detail
Subprime Bite
----
By Jason Leow
Bank of China Ltd. and the Industrial & Commercial Bank of China Ltd., two of China's largest lenders and big
investors in U.S. subprime assets, are scheduled to make a long-awaited announcement when they report
earnings Tuesday: how much their subprime exposure has eaten into the banks' profits.
China's banks have generally been much less exposed to the global slowdown and to subprime woes. Bank
of China, which historically has invested overseas, may be the exception in a record year for China's banks.
Due to its larger subprime exposure, Bank of China likely will report far-lower profits than will ICBC. Bank of
China's 2007 profits are forecast to increase 25% at best from the previous year. That is worse than its peers,
which are forecasting profit increases of at least 50%.
That isn't really awful in the context of what is going on around the world for financial institutions, said
Charlene Chu, a senior director at Fitch Ratings China. "The problem is, 2007 happened to be a stellar year
for earnings growth for most other Chinese banks," she said.
Bank of China has disclosed the value of its subprime investments only up until September. Analysts think
those investments may have deteriorated and have lost more value in the fourth quarter, so they are waiting
to hear if the bank has written down or set aside additional impairment charges for that period. These
write-downs and provisions will reduce its profit.
By September, the bank still had almost $8 billion in subprime-linked assets. Analysts believe its net income
will increase anywhere from 1% to 25%, though last month Chairman Xiao Gang was quoted by state-run
Xinhua news agency as saying the bank will report a "visible increase" in its 2007 profit.
Subprime exposure won't be the only drag on profits. The bank has large foreign exchange-denominated
holdings that have lost value, so it likely will have to record less income from those sources, too.
ICBC's profit for 2007 is forecast to rise 63% from a year earlier. Analysts are less concerned about its
smaller subprime exposure. In its third-quarter report, ICBC said it held subprime-mortgage bonds of about
$1.2 billion.
Analysts are more worried about how Chinese banks will perform this year. China's banks earn a sizable
portion of income from interest and fees, and both increased last year. Increased lending to corporate clients
helped banks post record interest incomes. A booming domestic stock market lifted sales of wealth-related
products and fueled fee income. Chinese customers also moved their savings from longer-term deposits into
short-term demand accounts in order to get out cash for buying stocks. That helped banks pay out less
interest returns.
But prospects for 2008 are dimmer. Given China's record inflation rate, the central bank looks set to tighten
the reins on loan growth. That will damp banks' interest earnings. Banks' fee-based income also will drop
from less investment activity. A slowing U.S. economy will exacerbate banks' earnings if customers start to
default on their loans.

--- Page 34 ---
Page 34 of 212 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Eventually, the banks' nonperforming-loan ratios may rise, though these are lagging indicators that don't
show up until months after an economy turns down. By December, the combined nonperforming-loan ratio of
Bank of China, ICBC, China Construction Bank Corp., Bank of Communications Co. and the Agricultural
Bank of China had risen to 8.05%, from 7.83% three months earlier, driven largely by the Agricultural Bank.
Zimbabwe:
Mugabe's Maneuvering Dims Hopes for Fair Election
----
By Sarah Childress
Anxious to ensure his victory in next Saturday's polls, the government of Zimbabwean President Robert
Mugabe has banned Western observers, intimidated the opposition and bribed starving rural dwellers with
food, international watchdog groups say.
All that has dimmed hope that despite international pressure and two strong opposition candidates, the
elections in Zimbabwe will be any fairer this time around than in previous years.
Still, the election will be the first time in Mr. Mugabe's 28-year rule that he will face a serious challenger from
within his own ranks. The president's former finance minister, Simba Makoni, is running against him, as is
longtime opposition leader Morgan Tsvangirai.
Mr. Tsvangirai, a dedicated human-rights activist and trade unionist, has endured beatings and intimidation
for opposing the government in previous elections. But his party, the Movement for Democratic Change, has
never succeeded in defeating Mr. Mugabe.
A chemist trained in the U.K., Mr. Makoni was fired from the government cabinet after criticizing the
president's economic policy. When he announced his candidacy in February, some outside observers and
Zimbabweans in the diaspora had held out hope that he might at least be able to loosen Mr. Mugabe's iron
grip on power. Mr. Makoni appeared to be backed by high-ranking members of the ruling party, the Zimbabwe
African National Unity-Patriotic Front.
He also had the support of a breakaway faction of the MDC, led by Arthur Mutambara, a businessman and
Rhodes Scholar. He threw his weight behind Mr. Makoni on the premise that a divided opposition would
guarantee Mr. Mugabe a win.
The Zimbabwean government agreed -- after negotiations brokered by South African president Thabo Mbeki
and the Southern African Development Community, a respected regional body -- to implement new guidelines
aimed at ensuring a free and fair election.
But hope that this poll would be different has waned as the brief campaign season comes to a close. The
government hasn't implemented the reforms and has banned observers from countries that it says are critical
of Mr. Mugabe, which includes all European nations.
"We do not expect a free and fair election," said Andebrhan Giorgis, senior adviser for the International Crisis
Group's Africa program. "We're hoping for the best, but that's hope against hope."
According to a report by Human Rights Watch, an independent group, Zanu-PF supporters have harassed
and beat up opposition supporters. The report also said that government and party officials have bribed rural
voters with food and farming equipment, and withheld it from those who weren't registered Zanu-PF
members. The government has dismissed the report, saying that Human Rights Watch is biased against
Zimbabwe.
Both opposition candidates have highlighted the economic devastation in Zimbabwe, a country rich in
platinum and gold but wrecked by corruption and mismanagement. Inflation is the highest in the world, and
people have little food or running water.
Yet Mr. Mugabe is still popular in rural areas, where access to unfiltered information is scarce and people still
remember the president when he first came to power in 1980 as a young revolutionary who overthrew white
supremacist rule.
In a bid to keep those votes, Mr. Mugabe signed a bill into law this month that will allow locals to take majority
shares in foreign companies. Analysts fear the populist move could further devastate the shattered economy,
similar to his 2000 decision to hand over white-run commercial farms to untrained black workers.
Supreme Court:

--- Page 35 ---
Page 35 of 212 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Mukasey to Jump
The Bar Tuesday
----
By Evan Perez
Appearing before the Supreme Court Tuesday will be a figure more accustomed to being on the other side of
the room: former federal judge and current Attorney General Michael Mukasey.
His appearance will reprise a tradition that was dispensed with by the last two Bush administration attorneys
general, John Ashcroft and Alberto Gonzales. Mr. Mukasey is set to argue the government's request to
overturn an appeals-court ruling in a terrorism case, and he acknowledges he is nervous.
Last fall, when he visited the court as part of his formal introduction to the job, he sat in on a case. "I watched
at least one judge interrupt what I thought was getting to be a responsive answer, with: 'Now answer my
question,'" he said. Reminded that Supreme Court judges are actually called justices, he deadpanned: "That's
something to remember."
Mr. Mukasey will be arguing the "millennium bomber" case, U.S. v. Ahmed Ressam. The government is
appealing a lower-court decision to strike one of the counts on which Mr. Ressam was convicted. Mr. Ressam
and an accomplice traveled by ferry from Canada to Washington state with plans to carry out a terror attack
on Los Angeles International Airport in December 1999. He was sentenced to 22 years on terror-conspiracy
charges.
The government argues the appeals court erred in its decision, which spares Mr. Ressam an extra 10 years
in prison for the explosives found in the car in which he was traveling.
Janet Reno, in 1996, was the last attorney general to argue a case before the nation's highest court, winning
a case that defended police procedures during traffic stops. Justices are known for not giving much deference
to attorneys general, treating them no better than other lawyers who often get interrupted as they try to deliver
arguments they spent weeks preparing.
To prepare for what Mr. Mukasey expects will be his first and only chance to appear before the nation's
highest court as the attorney general, he has been holding moot-court sessions. "Unlike Walter Dellinger [the
Duke University law professor who recently argued the District of Columbia's case defending its gun-ban law]
I haven't had the guts to invite people to watch," the attorney general said during a meeting with reporters at
Justice Department headquarters.
On the bench, Mr. Mukasey was known for sometimes having a stern and efficient demeanor, particularly if
lawyers appeared to be rambling in their arguments. He joked that some lawyers who appeared before him
are "going to be lining up in the street waiting for tickets" for a chance to savor watching him get interrupted
by the justices.
The attorney general said he jumped at the chance when he learned he could have the opportunity to argue a
case before the Supreme Court. He didn't pick which case to argue and said this one fit his schedule. Did he
have enough time to prepare? "I guess we'll find out on Tuesday," he said.

Dollar Pushes Higher Again
Currency Trading
Dollar Pushes Higher Again
By Min Zeng
523 words
21 March 2008
The Wall Street Journal
J
C14
English
Copyright 2008 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
The beleaguered dollar got energized by a continued selloff in commodities that fueled its strong rebound
against major rivals including the euro.
The rally began Wednesday as crude oil and gold prices nose-dived from record highs set at the start of the
week. Talk of investors selling liquid assets to make up for losses from soured bets, as well as profit-taking
ahead of Easter, sparked more selling in commodities Thursday and crushed currencies whose fortunes are
closely tied to these markets.
Currencies from Australia, New Zealand, South Africa and Canada were among the hardest hit; the
Australian dollar fell below 90 U.S. cents, while the Canadian dollar reached its lowest point since January.
The euro, which has surged sharply during the past few weeks against the dollar, at one point fell below
$1.54, a big drop from the record high of $1.5905 it set Monday.
"It is deleveraging day in currency land," said Paresh Upadhyaya, who helps manage $29 billion in currency
assets at Putnam Investments in Boston. "People are reducing risks leading into the long weekend.
"With all these credit-market problems and slowing growth in the U.S., there is a big question mark whether
global growth is going to be sustained," Mr. Upadhyaya said. "Something has to give."
Late Thursday, the euro was $1.5417 from $1.5609 late Wednesday, while the dollar was 99.19 yen from
98.98 yen. The euro was 152.91 yen from 154.50 yen. The British pound was at $1.9838 from $1.9817, and
the dollar was at 1.0115 Swiss francs from 1.0005 francs.
The dollar got an extra boost in the afternoon after U.S. stocks surged as the Federal Reserve announced the
details of its first term security lending facility, in which the central bank will lend Treasurys to dealer banks in
exchange for a broad array of mortgage-related collateral.
That helped the dollar gain against the yen. But the Japanese currency outperformed the euro and other
major currencies as the coming long weekend and the selloff in commodities also spurred investors to unwind
popular carry trades.
In that process, investors who have borrowed from low-rate Japan to fund high-yielding investments
elsewhere had to buy back yen to repay some of the loans. Buying in yen was so heavy that it pushed the
euro below 152 yen.
Brian Dolan, chief currency strategist at Forex.com, a unit of online currency trading firm Gain Capital in
Bedminster, N.J., said that of all the major currencies, the yen is the safest investment at the moment, as the
Japanese financial sector appears to have little subprime exposure.
The Swiss franc, another popular funding currency for the carry trade, plunged against the dollar after Credit
Suisse Group warned that it was likely to post a first-quarter loss.
The British pound erased losses against the dollar after an unexpected surge in February's retail sales
dashed hopes of an interest-rate cut next month, helping sterling limit its decline after a steep downfall a day
ago.

Hong Kong Shares Slide 3.5% On Pullback in Commodities --- Europe's Stocks Are Dragged Down By Credit Suisse
World Stock Markets
Hong Kong Shares Slide 3.5% On Pullback in Commodities --- Europe's Stocks Are Dragged Down By
Credit Suisse
By V. Phani Kumar and Steve Goldstein

Dow Rallies 261.66 Points On Financials --- But the Risks of Selloffs Keep Investors Cautious;

The financial sector led a furious stock-market rally as banks and brokerages snapped back from
Wednesday's selloff, while commodities prices continued their slide.
The Dow Jones Industrial Average retraced nearly all of Wednesday's 293-point loss to end the day 261.66
points higher, up 2.2%, at 12361.32. It was the Dow's third straight move of at least 200 points, led by jumps
of more than 8% in its components Citigroup, J.P. Morgan Chase and Bank of America. and
The industrials are now off 6.8% this year and 12.7% below their October record close.
But even on days with big gains, many investing professionals remain cautious about the market's prospects
over the next few months. Many say recent moves by the Federal Reserve, including the three-quarter-point
cut in its key interest-rate target Tuesday and its lending to investment banks, have helped calm the markets.
Still, the financial sector -- a bellwether for the broader market -- remains at risk of selloffs amid concerns
about further write-downs of bad credit bets, which could spark further volatility.
"The Fed has been very creative, and it has done a good job of getting risk capital on the move again," said
strategist Stephen Wood, of asset-management firm Russell Investment Group in New York. "But as long as
risk capital is on the move, it can leave a particular area of the market just as quickly as it comes in."
The Standard & Poor's 500-stock index gained 2.4%, or 31.09 points, at 1329.51, off 9.5% on the year and
down 15.1% from its own October record. The broad measure was led by a 7.2% jump in its financial sector.
Among bellwether names in the sector, Fannie Mae shares soared 12% and Freddie Mac was up 9% after
analysts at Keefe, Bruyette & Woods upgraded both to the equivalent of a "buy" rating from "hold," saying
that the recent easing of regulatory constraints on the mortgage-finance giants' lending should allow them to
contribute to increased activity in the broader mortgage arena.
But in a reminder of the risks still facing financials, CIT Group said it drew on its $7.3 billion in bank credit
lines, as it struggled to refinance debt that will be due in the next few months. The finance company's shares
plunged 17%.
Options and futures contracts expired, contributing to heavy trading volume. More than six billion New York
Stock Exchange-listed shares changed hands.
The commodity-price downturn continued, with the broad Dow Jones-AIG Commodity Index falling 2%. Gold
futures tumbled $25.10 to $919.60 an ounce. Oil futures, a popular target for speculators in recent weeks,
fell 70 cents to $101.84 a barrel.
"We've all recognized that [oil has] run way past its fundamentals," said commodities broker Mike Fitzpatrick,
at MF Global in New York. He estimated that supply and demand, which is softening along with U.S.
economic growth, warrant a price around $86.
The dollar rose against the euro and the yen. The dollar briefly recaptured the 100 yen level but later slipped
to 99.19 yen, compared to 98.98 yen late Wednesday.
A stronger dollar tends to hurt commodity prices, which are traded globally in dollar terms. As the currency
strengthens, buyers need fewer dollars to obtain the same amount of raw materials they used before.
The availability of liquidity, or a critical mass of buyers and sellers needed to guarantee the smooth
functioning of markets, has worried traders and analysts for weeks. They have complained that even players

--- Page 42 ---

who have sufficient collateral on hand often shy away from conducting trades out of sheer worry, not because
they can't afford it on paper.
Hope has spread this week that situation may be getting better, although there may yet be a few nasty
surprises at individual companies. The Dow ended the holiday-shortened trading week up 3.4%, its best week
this month.
"People forget that financial crises tend not to last long," said Neil Hennessy, president of Hennessy Funds in
