Commodities Lose Some Of Their Luster --- Broad Selloff in Gold, Oil Underscores Risk for Investors Who Piled In Seeking Safety
Commodities Lose Some Of Their Luster --- Broad Selloff in Gold, Oil Underscores Risk for Investors
Who Piled In Seeking Safety
By Carolyn Cui
1,066 words
20 March 2008
The Wall Street Journal
J
D1
English
Copyright 2008 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
It's a risky time for small investors to put their money into commodities, as yesterday's broad selloff shows.
Despite numerous bullish forecasts -- and a long-term rally that has seen record highs for gold and oil -- the
sector remains volatile.
Oil, gold and grain markets all took a dive yesterday, as investors bet that the Federal Reserve's
lower-than-expected interest-rate cut would calm fears of an inflationary spiral. This, in turn, lessened the
appeal of hard assets, whose prices tend to rise when inflation picks up, and underscores the markets'
vulnerability to further tumbles.
Although plenty of traders and analysts think raw materials will continue their multiyear run, a recent flood of
investor cash into the sector -- and yesterday's shakeout -- serve as warning signs that new money could exit
as quickly as it came.
It is often small investors who come late to the party, when prices are already lofty. Several analysts say
newcomers, both institutions and individual investors, are responsible for pushing up prices to
higher-than-predicted levels this year for everything from crude oil to copper and palladium as economic
news turned ever bleaker.
Adarsh Viswanathan, 32, a product manager at a high-tech company, is one of those newbies. He sold his
financial stocks in December and bought Agriculture Total Return, an exchange-traded note tracking the
RICI-Agriculture Index, as insurance against inflation. Since he bought it, the note has gone up about 11%,
and Mr. Viswanathan, of Santa Clara, Calif., is considering increasing his commodities exposure.
The commodities market "took a beating because it has gone too high over the last few months," he says.
"But if you look at the long-term cycle, the bullishness can only get stronger, because China, India and East
Europe -- they are all going to modernize."
Yet many participants aren't in it for the long haul, as this week's wild swings showed. Crude oil on the New
York Mercantile Exchange dropped 4.1% Monday, or $4.53 per barrel, only to surge 3.5% on Tuesday.
Similarly, gasoline reversed a 6.9% plunge on Monday with a 6.2% gain Tuesday.
Then, yesterday, commodities fell again across the board -- with oil falling 4.5%, or $4.94 per barrel, its
biggest dollar drop in over 17 years, and gold taking an especially deep dive of $58.50, or 5.8%. Gold's drop
was its biggest decline in dollar terms since Jan. 22, 1980.
There are many reasons for the big run-up in commodities over the past few months. Professional investors
normally active in the now-jittery credit and stock markets, for one, have been looking to park money
elsewhere while they ride out storms in those sectors. Oil and gold are easy picks as a hedge against
inflation.
Individuals, too, have been trying their hand at commodities in greater numbers, aided by easy-to-use new
investment tools such as exchange-traded notes and funds -- which trade on exchanges like stocks and can
hold hard assets directly -- and commodities-focused mutual funds. The weak dollar has also helped to push
prices higher, since commodities markets are denominated in dollars and it has been taking more of them to
buy hard assets.
Bob Milsom, a 63-year-old retiree in Washington, got interested in corn recently, thinking the growing use of
ethanol will give corn prices a push. He signed up for a grains newsletter from MF Global Ltd., a futures and
options broker, a few weeks ago and started following the crop. Now Mr. Milsom says he's "overwhelmed by
the market movement" and is leery about jumping in.

--- Page 44 ---
Page 44 of 212 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Peter Sorrentino, portfolio manager of the commodity-focused Huntington Real Strategies Fund, warns that
"there's so much money pouring into commodities right now, it [was] lifting them all uniformly. And I don't think
the fundamentals are supporting them all." Mr. Sorrentino says many investors have been shying from stock
bets until they believe that market has bottomed. In commodities, he sees a potential "mass exodus, as this
flow of money either stops or regresses itself back into other investment tools."
Other big risks loom. Although commodities have so far defied gloomy economic forecasts, a deeper global
recession could snuff out some of the robust new demand from developing countries.
What's more, banks that lend money to trading clients to finance their commodities bets are taking new steps
to shore up their cash cushions amid the global credit crunch. Over the past few weeks, many brokerage
houses and trade-clearing agents have required or are considering requiring futures traders to post more
collateral, or margin, to cover the daily fluctuations in the value of their bets, traders say.
If investors don't have enough cash to meet the margin requirements, they might have to close out some
positions to come up with more money -- which could drive prices down further.
Futures exchanges, which often clear, or stand behind, trades between parties, are also raising their margin
requirements. The Chicago Board of Trade, the Kansas City Board of Trade and the Minneapolis Grain
Exchange in the past month have raised minimum margins for wheat futures due to the market's increased
volatility.
Yesterday's big drops also showed that profit-taking at these levels is tempting. In the week ended March 12,
mutual funds and exchange-traded funds investing in commodities futures and raw-material stocks
commanding total assets of $65 billion saw a net outflow of $485 million, the largest weekly drop this year,
according to EPFR Global, a research firm tracking global fund flows and asset allocation.
"The biggest risk is when speculators are all lined up on one side," as they are now, says Peter Kordell,
director of research at Jigsaw Commodities, a Minneapolis-based commodities broker. "When it comes to an
end, it's vicious because everybody is running for the door at the same time."
---
Hard Times For Hard Assets?
Yesterday's selloff shows how volatile commodities can be. Here are two
reasons why prices, after a long run-up, may be poised to fall further:
Some believe that fundamental demand doesn't support such lofty prices.
A global recession could cut demand for raw materials.

Gold, Oil Sag, as Dollar Firms --- Funds Moving Out After Recent Rally; Demand Weakens
Commodities Report
Gold, Oil Sag, as Dollar Firms --- Funds Moving Out After Recent Rally; Demand Weakens
By Gregory Meyer and Allen Sykora
538 words
20 March 2008
The Wall Street Journal
J
C8
English
Copyright 2008 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Crude oil and gold each suffered steep declines, as a slightly stronger dollar put commodities to rout.
March gold fell $58.50, or 5.8%, to $944.70 an ounce on the Comex division of the New York Mercantile
Exchange. This was the largest one-day fall since a $143.50 collapse in January 1980, when the metal was
especially volatile and hit records that stood until early this year.
Light, sweet crude oil for April delivery, a contract that expired yesterday, settled $4.94, or 4.5%, lower at
$104.48 a barrel on the Nymex. This drop was the steepest in dollar terms since January 1991, when the
U.S. launched its operation to oust Iraqi troops from Kuwait and simultaneously opened its strategic
petroleum reserve.
"What we're really seeing is complete shift of funds out of the commodity markets," said Mike Zarembski,
senior commodity analyst at brokerage firm optionsExpress Inc. In Chicago. "My quote board here is all red."
Over the past weeks, commodities prices have surged across the board as the dollar weakened against other
major currencies. A weak dollar gives foreign buyers greater purchasing power, which gives them greater
latitude in bidding up prices. A weaker dollar has also raised the specter of inflation, a boost to gold, which
historically has served as a hedge against broad-based price increases.
Gold closed firmer Tuesday ahead of the Federal Reserve's cut of benchmark interest rates by
three-quarters of a percentage point. However, prices began to soften in the after-hours trade following the
decision and those losses snowballed.
As the cut was smaller than some had expected, the dollar began to appreciate, a signal for some investors
to take profits. The topsy-turvy nature of the market made most participants hesitant to call yesterday's action
the beginnings of a major pullback.
"We're not flinching today," said Scott Meyers, senior trading analyst at Pioneer Futures in New York. "It's
commodity prices. We've come to accept these kinds of moves as not crazy."
Both oil and gold also were pulled down by developments in the physical markets. U.S. oil demand
weakened in the last four weeks compared with the year-earlier period, according to U.S. government data
for the week ended Friday. India, a large gold consumer, imported significantly less gold last year than a
year earlier due to rising prices.
In other commodity markets:
SOYBEANS: Prices fell to their daily exchange-imposed limit of 50 cents per bushel, pressured by
speculators liquidating bullish positions to take profit amid the U.S. economy's uncertainties. Soybeans were
hit by further tightening of bank credit for hedge funds, weakness in Asian vegetable oil markets that compete
with soybeans and a firmer U.S. dollar, pulling the rest of the grain complex lower. Chicago Board of Trade
May soybeans settled 50 cents lower at $12.57 a bushel.
COFFEE: Futures fell in a commodity-wide break as speculators pulled back on bullish positions. ICE Futures
U.S. coffee May coffee fell 4.60 cents to $1.3295 a pound.

Japan's Stock Rally to Nowhere? --- Tune In, Tokyo: Government Is Proving Impediment to Sustained Gains
World Stock Markets
Japan's Stock Rally to Nowhere? --- Tune In, Tokyo: Government Is Proving Impediment to Sustained
Gains
By Sebastian Moffett
1,100 words
20 March 2008
The Wall Street Journal
J
C7
English
Copyright 2008 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Tokyo -- Tokyo's stock rally yesterday is unlikely to be sustained. Before Japan has a long stock advance, an
increasing number of analysts are saying, clear signs are needed that the country is going to be
better-governed.
The Nikkei Stock Average rose 2.5% yesterday to close at 12260.44, after the big U.S. interest-rate cut eased
concerns over U.S. demand for Japanese products. Japanese stocks are still down about 30% since June.
That makes Japan the worst performer among major world markets, in spite of the relatively small direct
damage on Japanese financial institutions from the U.S. subprime-mortgage debacle.
Japanese stocks are now cheap by world standards. One measure showing this is price-to-book-value ratios,
or PBRs, calculated by dividing a company's share price by its net assets. The average Japanese PBR is
1.45, the lowest of major economies, according to S&P Citigroup World Indices. That of U.S. stocks is 2.53.
On Monday, the PBRs of nearly three-fifths of the companies trading on the first section of the Tokyo Stock
Exchange were less than one -- meaning it would be more profitable for shareholders to liquidate the
company and divide the assets among themselves.
The trouble is, such action to get more value out of a company is hard in Japan, where companies make
elaborate efforts to avoid getting taken over. In the past, that usually meant developing webs of
cross-shareholdings, in which companies hold mutual stakes -- usually when they have some kind of
business relationship.
Now, some companies use other techniques, such as so-called poison pills, to dilute the stake of an
unwanted suitor. Some analysts date the start of Japan's stock-price declines to an incident in June, when
condiment maker Bull-Dog Sauce adopted such a strategy to prevent a takeover by Steel Partners Japan
Strategic Fund (Offshore), a fund run by U.S. investors. A Tokyo court declared the defense legal.
"Japan . . . has no market for corporate control," said Patrick Mohr, a strategist at Nikko Citigroup. "You don't
have an active M&A market. People can't come in and take a company out for lots of different reasons,
mainly cultural."
He said investors are waiting for a major event signaling that Japan will have more merger-and-acquisition
activity and find ways to increase corporate value. This could come in the form of a landmark takeover -- of
one of the country's giant electronics conglomerates, for example. Some of these possess cutting-edge
technology, but their earnings are dragged down by other, less-competitive divisions.
Another such event might be the emergence of a leader such as pro-market former Prime Minister Junichiro
Koizumi. The stock market soared under Mr. Koizumi as he pushed economic deregulation and other moves
to shrink the size of the government. (Political analysts have said a comeback by the popular Mr. Koizumi is
unlikely.)
But instead of some such positive signal, investors are getting a series of indications that the government is
even weaker than they thought.
For the second time in a week, Prime Minister Yasuo Fukuda yesterday suffered parliamentary rejection of a
candidate he had nominated to succeed Toshihiko Fukui as head of the Bank of Japan. This has left the
country without a central-bank governor, as of Thursday.
While the operations of the bank will likely be unaffected -- another member of the board can become acting
governor until a new one is found -- economists said the fiasco illustrates serious flaws in the way Japan is
governed.

--- Page 51 ---
Page 51 of 212 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
First, it shows the current administration is weak. Mr. Fukuda miscalculated in his tactics in dealing with the
opposition Democratic Party of Japan, which controls Parliament's upper house and so has veto power over
some personnel appointments, including that of the Bank of Japan governor.
He bet that the opposition party would approve his nominations in order to avoid being seen as responsible
for a vacancy at the top of the central bank. But the opposition rejected Mr. Fukuda's candidates, anyway,
making him look ineffectual.
"The prime minister's authority has declined, which means that Fukuda's time in office will be shortened," said
Morgan Stanley economist Takehiro Sato.
Second, the trouble over the Bank of Japan governor points to problems with Japan's more fundamental legal
and constitutional setup. For example, one way to ensure that an impasse over confirmation didn't result in a
vacancy would have been to let the incumbent bank governor stay in office until Parliament approved a
successor.
Mr. Fukui wasn't allowed to remain beyond Wednesday, as the bank's laws weren't designed to cope with a
Parliament where different political groupings controlled different chambers.
European Indexes Decline
On Finance-Sector Jitters
European shares fell Wednesday as persistent worries about the financial sector hammered United
Kingdom-listed mortgage banks and a plunge in the price of gold sank miners.
Telefon AB L.M. Ericsson skidded 7.8% in Stockholm after its mobile-phone-making joint venture with Sony
issued a profit warning. Fears of an industry-wide slowdown sent shares of competitor Nokia down 5.9%.
Those losses helped make the technology sector the worst-performing area of the Dow Jones Stoxx 600.
Overall, the pan-European Dow Jones Stoxx 600 index fell 1% to 297.48.
In LONDON, the FTSE 100 fell 1.1% to 5545.60. HBOS ended down 7.1% after declining as much as 17% on
speculation about its financial health. The shares recovered some of the losses after the U.K.'s biggest
mortgage lender denied liquidity problems.
Miners fell as prices for gold, copper and other metals skidded. Copper specialist Antofagasta lost 4.9%, and
platinum miner Lonmin dropped 6.9%.
In FRANKFURT, the DAX 30 lost 0.5% to 6361.22. An update from Deutsche Telekom disappointed
investors, dragging shares of Europe's largest telecommunications company down 6.9%. The company said
revenue at its T-Home German fixed-line and broadband division would fall 4% to 6% this year and adjusted
operating profit is seen declining 5% to 8%. Some traders interpreted the targets as a profit warning, although
the company kept its 2008 outlook.
In PARIS, the CAC-40 declined 0.6% to 4555.95. BNP Paribas rose 4.7%, in part because it ruled out a bid
for rival French bank Societe Generale, which fell 7.1%.

Ahead of the Tape
Ahead of the Tape
Ahead of the Tape
By Mark Gongloff
562 words
20 March 2008
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2008 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Is the Bubble
In Commodities
Bursting?
While trying to clean up the mess of one bubble this week, the Fed might also have unexpectedly hastened
the end of another.
Oil, gold and other commodity prices have soared recently, much higher than what would seem to be
fundamentally justified. They took a big tumble in the other direction yesterday, with gold posting its biggest
dollar decline in 28 years and oil its biggest loss in 17 years.
One cause of the selling seemed to be Tuesday's interest-rate decision by the Fed, which didn't lower rates
as much as markets expected. Moreover, in its statement accompanying the cuts, it spent a surprisingly large
amount of time harrumphing about inflation risks. That seems to have eased concerns that the Fed was
prepared to toss out its inflation-fighting mandate.
Hedge funds and other investors that had pumped cash -- much of it borrowed -- into commodities might now
be having second thoughts. If the Fed is serious about fighting inflation and will be more reluctant to cut
rates in the future, then the run-up in commodities prices might be running its course. Moreover, if the
economy gets weak enough to damp inflation on its own, that's also poison for commodities, as it means
slumping demand for oil, steel, wheat and other hard assets.
The deleveraging process gripping Wall Street is another important factor. If the downdraft in commodities
prices continues, hedge funds and other traders that made bets on raw materials with borrowed money could
be forced to sell their holdings and unwind those bets.
A commodities meltdown would be good news for consumers, who've had enough of high gas prices. But it
might not help the financial sector. For many banks and investors, commodities investments had been the
only thing going up lately, besides delinquencies.
The FedEx Indicator
Is Pointing South
Transportation companies like FedEx offer a window into the pernicious effects of a slow economy and rising
commodities costs.
Slow growth and high energy costs seem to have squeezed FedEx's third-quarter earnings, due to be
reported today. Analysts surveyed by Thomson Financial expect it to post net income of $1.22 a share, down
10% from a year ago.
Rival UPS warned last week that it could miss first-quarter earnings targets after six straight weeks of
negative volume in its U.S. package business. Kurt Kuehn, UPS's chief financial officer, said "across a very
broad segment of our customers there is a slowing in shipping needs."
High energy prices and weakening demand have also thumped truckers. The American Trucking
Associations expects truckers to spend $135 billion on fuel this year, up 20% from 2007. Several trucking
companies describe the environment today as the worst they've seen, say Morgan Keegan analysts.

--- Page 54 ---
Page 54 of 212 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Trouble in transports signals a bad outlook more broadly. FedEx shares tumbled in 1999, before the rest of
the market. The " FedEx Indicator" seems to be working well again. Its shares started dropping in July and
are down 26% since then. The Dow Jones Industrial Average didn't start slipping until October.
Lower commodities prices would help the transports now. But energy costs would have to fall a lot before
really sinking in.
-- Scott Patterson

Commodity Declines Aid Dollar
Currency Trading
Commodity Declines Aid Dollar
By Min Zeng
434 words
20 March 2008
The Wall Street Journal
J
C16
English
Copyright 2008 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
A selloff in commodities and a declining British pound helped the dollar recoup its early losses against the
euro.
Investors have sold the dollar heavily during the past few weeks to buy hard assets such as gold and oil, and
now they are selling those positions, generating a wave of short-covering in their bets against the dollar. Gold
shed 5.8%, and crude oil fell 4.5%, amid talk of hedge funds and banks forced to sell hard assets to meet
margin calls and deal with funding difficulties.
Commodity-linked currencies, including Australia's dollar, New Zealand's dollar, Canada's dollar and South
Africa's rand were all under water against the U.S. currency yesterday.
The British pound suffered throughout the day against the dollar and the euro, hammered by speculation of
interest-rate cuts and worries about liquidity issues in local financial markets.
The yen, however, was on solid ground against the dollar and the euro as tumbling U.S. stocks triggered risk
aversion and unwinding of the popular carry trade. In that process, investors who had borrowed in
low-yielding yen to fund high-yielding investments elsewhere had to buy back yen to repay some of their
loans. Japan's borrowing cost, at 1%, is the lowest among industrialized nations.
Late yesterday, the euro was at $1.5609 from $1.5636 Tuesday, while the dollar was at 98.98 yen from 99.56
yen. The euro was at 154.50 yen from 155.67 yen. The pound fell to $1.9817 from $2.0081; the dollar was at
1.0005 Swiss francs from 1.0017.
Just Monday, the dollar had struck a record low of $1.5904 per euro.
"Much of the dollar recovery is on the back of weaker commodity prices and the further unwinding of
European currencies prompted by the selling off in [sterling/dollar] as a result of credit fears stemming from
U.K. banks," said Matthew Kassel, director of proprietary trading at ING Financial Markets LLC in New York.
The dollar started to move off its lows of the day after better-than-expected earnings from Morgan Stanley
and the clearance of federally chartered Fannie Mae and Freddie Mac to buy more home loans.
Then, the selloff in commodities, coupled with a tumbling British pound, helped the dollar recover further from
its overnight decline.
In Shanghai, a record low for the dollar-yuan central parity and the central bank's increase in the reserve
requirement ratio pushed the Chinese currency to a modern-era high against the dollar.

Crude Oil Jumps 3.5% As Hedge Players Return
Commodities Report
Crude Oil Jumps 3.5% As Hedge Players Return
By Brian Baskin
484 words
19 March 2008
The Wall Street Journal
J
C9
English
Copyright 2008 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Crude-oil futures ended 3.5% higher after the Federal Reserve's interest-rate reduction prompted a
late-session surge.
Light, sweet crude for April delivery settled at $109.42 a barrel on the New York Mercantile Exchange, a gain
of $3.74 for the day. The contract expires today. The day's gains nearly erased Monday's drop, which was the
biggest one-day percentage loss since August.
Futures rose by about $2 yesterday morning and spent most of the day at that level, with activity light as the
market awaited the Fed's decision, which came just 15 minutes before the close. The three-quarter
pecentage-point cut failed to match widely held expectations of a full-point move. Nevertheless, crude soared
above $109 on the news -- in large part because investors drawn to the market by monetary policy had
nowhere else to go, said Addison Armstrong, an analyst with TFS Energy in Stamford, Conn.
"The funds who are clearly in charge of these markets really don't have any other way to play it at this point,"
Mr. Armstrong said. "They can't find a return anywhere else other than commodities markets, where there's
momentum and there's liquidity."
The Fed's moves are closely watched by commodities traders for their effect on the dollar. Cuts to interest
rates tend to weaken the U.S. currency, which in turn tends to buoy oil prices as investors seeking a hedge
flood into commodities.
The dollar weakened slightly against the euro and the yen after the cut was announced, but later
strengthened.
The Fed's actions may have given the crude-futures market renewed strength, enough to overcome
Monday's big drop and resume the two-month-old rally, said Peter Donovan, a vice president at Vantage
Trading in New York. "This is definitely going to put [talk of a correction] on the back burner," Mr. Donovan
said.
The market's attention now turns to the Department of Energy's weekly oil and product inventory data, which
is expected today. Crude-oil and gasoline stockpiles are expected to have increased in the week ended last
Friday, while analysts forecast that distillates, which include diesel and heating oil, decreased.
In other commodity markets:
GOLD: Futures were modestly firmer, but was largely range-bound as the market awaited the outcome of a
meeting of the Federal Open Market Committee, traders said. April gold rose on the Comex division of the
Nymex $1.70 an ounce to $1,004.30. In the thinly traded after-market session, gold futures fell, slipping back
under $1,000 after the Fed rate cut.
COFFEE: Prices rose off the steep losses incurred Monday, as speculators bought while other commodities
rose. ICE Futures U.S. May arabica coffee settled up 1.55 cents to $1.3755 a pound.

Credit Worries Ease as Fed Cuts, Hints at More Relief --- Stocks Jump 3.5% In Rally Reflecting Cautious Optimism
Credit Worries Ease as Fed Cuts, Hints at More Relief --- Stocks Jump 3.5% In Rally Reflecting
Cautious Optimism
By Greg Ip
1,570 words
19 March 2008
The Wall Street Journal
J
A1
English
Copyright 2008 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
WASHINGTON -- The Federal Reserve continued its two-front attack on the credit crunch with a steep rate
cut, and hinted at more to come.
The cut was less than financial markets wanted. But in a sign the Fed's prior efforts to boost lending through
unconventional means may be getting some traction, stocks soared, buoyed by earnings reports from two big
investment banks.
The Fed cut its short-term interest-rate target 0.75 percentage point to 2.25%, not the full point that markets
had been expecting. It was the largest disappointment the Fed has delivered to markets since the central
bank began cutting rates in September. the move was also a signal that because of the Fed's concerns
about inflation, it expects its other initiatives to bear more of the burden of stimulating growth.
For at least a day, investors cheered. The Dow Jones Industrial Average rose 300 points ahead of the rate
cut on a growing conviction the Fed's prior efforts to funnel cash to credit markets would work. Though stocks
lost a chunk of their gains right after the 2:15 p.m. rate-cut announcement, they surged back to close up
420.41 points, or 3.5%, at 12392.66.
Financial stocks did especially well. Shares of Lehman Brothers Holdings, under sharp pressure lately,
soared more than 46%. Goldman Sachs shares rose over 16%. Both reported first-quarter earnings that,
while off more than 50% from a year ago, were better than expected, prompting descriptions of the market
surge as a relief rally.
The dollar improved against both the euro and Japanese yen after the Fed announcement. The U.S. currency
has fallen sharply in recent weeks as global investors questioned the Fed's commitment to resisting inflation,
a worry that has sent money pouring into gold, oil and other inflation hedges. Oil sold off briefly after the
announcement but then rose to close up $3.74 a barrel at $109.42.
Though less than expected, the interest-rate cut was substantial. Yesterday was only the second time the
Fed has cut so much since it began announcing its rate moves in 1994. The central bank now has cut
short-term interest rates a full three points since September, the fastest pace of easing in decades.
This reflects the speed with which a slump in housing and subprime mortgages has spiraled into a broad
credit crunch, weakening financial institutions and making it harder for a broad swath of businesses and
households to borrow. Most economists now think the U.S. is in a recession, with the debate shifting to how
deep it will be.
Recognizing that, the Fed signaled in its end-of-meeting statement that the prospect of more rate cuts
remains on the table. "The outlook . . . has weakened further," it said. "Consumer spending has slowed and
labor markets have softened. Financial markets remain under considerable stress, and the tightening of credit
conditions and the deepening of the housing contraction are likely to weigh on economic growth." It said
"downside risks to growth remain," and the Fed will "act in a timely manner as needed."
Nonetheless, the Fed has increasingly come to the view that lower rates alone won't restore order to the
financial markets and prevent a severe recession. It has rolled out ever more creative and aggressive
attempts to infuse cash into market corners where it normally doesn't operate, culminating in Sunday's
decision to lend to investment banks from its "discount window," a privilege previously reserved for
commercial banks. Chairman Ben Bernanke also has publicly backed action to use public money to stem a
tide of mortgage defaults and foreclosures.
The Fed's actions are among several aggressive steps throughout the federal government that are coming to
a head this week and could prove critical in combating the crisis. Today the regulator of Fannie Mae and
Freddie Mac, which provide the bulk of funding for home mortgages, is to announce an easing of their capital

--- Page 60 ---
Page 60 of 212 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
requirements and the companies are to pledge to raise more capital, people familiar with the matter said.
Those steps should enable them to back more mortgages.
Meanwhile, the Bush Administration which has so far resisted using large amounts of public money to save
borrowers and lenders from bad loans, may be ready to compromise with Democrats on a more activist
approach to the housing crisis. The Fed is "running out of pages in its playbook to address the growing crisis
of credit and confidence," Senate Banking Committee Chairman Chris Dodd, who has proposed the Federal
Housing Administration insure up to $400 billion in troubled mortgages, said yesterday. Now the Bush
administration should be "equally aggressive."
Yesterday, the Fed expressed more concern about prices than after its Jan. 30 meeting. "Inflation has been
elevated, and some indicators of inflation expectations have risen," it said. While the Fed still expects
inflation to ease as commodity prices plateau and as higher unemployment blunts wage and price gains, it
said that "uncertainty about the inflation outlook has increased. It will be necessary to continue to monitor
inflation developments carefully."
With the economy continuing to weaken, the Fed remains concerned about what some officials describe as
an "adverse feedback loop" of economic weakness, tighter lending and further weakness. It is thus likely to
cut interest rates again.
But the elevated concern about inflation in the statement suggests the Fed does think it's getting close to the
bottom in interest rates. Some analysts speculate the Fed could trim rates before its next scheduled meeting
on April 29-30, but many think that's unlikely unless the outlook or markets get markedly worse.
The futures markets reduced the expectation they are showing for additional cuts. The futures now see the
federal-funds rate, charged on overnight loans between banks, easing to between 1.5% and 1.75% by year's
end. Before yesterday's move, futures investors were betting the rate would fall to 1.25% to 1.5%.
The Fed also lowered the rate charged on direct "discount window" loans it makes available to banks and --
since Sunday -- to investment banks. It slashed the rate to 2.5% from 3.25%, keeping it a quarter-point above
the federal-funds rate. Commercial banks began lowering their prime interest rates, to which some
variable-rate loans are tied, to 5.25% from 6%.
Highlighting the depth of the Fed's inflation concerns, two of the 10 voting members of the policy-setting
Federal Open Market Committee voted against the 3/4-point cut. Charles Plosser and Richard Fisher,
presidents of the Federal Reserve Banks of Philadelphia and Dallas, "preferred less aggressive action at this
meeting," the Fed said.
A worsening of both inflation and economic growth in the past month has made FOMC decisions more
fractious. Each of the past five rate-cut decisions has triggered at least one dissent -- one favoring a bigger
cut and the others favoring less. Yesterday's was the first meeting with two dissents since 2002.
Messrs. Plosser and Fisher have been the most outspoken about inflation risks, but most other Fed
policymakers are also uncomfortable with the recent deterioration in inflation psychology. The decision to
trim the federal-fund rate less than markets expected appears, at least in part, a way to convey both that the
Fed isn't panicking and that the central bank is weighing consequences of its actions on inflation
psychology.
The economy is hardly out of the woods. Investor confidence remains fragile just days after the Fed made an
unprecedented loan to prevent the collapse of Bear Stearns Cos., exposing how deeply the credit squeeze
had penetrated the U.S. financial system. Sunday's statement that the Fed would lend to investment banks,
and on the same terms as to commercial banks, was the boldest step the central bank has taken so far to
employ its own balance sheet to take some riskier debt off the hands of financial institutions.
The stresses in the credit markets have kept some of the Fed's rate-cutting efforts from filtering through to
borrowers. The average rate on 30-year mortgages fell to 5.5% in December from 6.4% in July when the
crisis began; but then the rate went above 6% in February, even though the Fed had been cutting short-term
rates since September.
After the Fed a week ago said it would lend up to $200 billion in Treasury securities to bond dealers in return
for mortgage-backed securities, mortgage rates fell to 5.74% from 6.2%, according to Bankrate.com.
Rates on "jumbo" mortgages above $417,000 -- until recently, the biggest that could be guaranteed by Fannie
Mae and Freddie Mac -- are higher now than in July, because banks have trouble finding investors to buy
securities backed by such loans. The Fed's move a week ago to lend to bond dealers also knocked down
rates on jumbo loans, to 7.02%.

--- Page 61 ---
Page 61 of 212 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Bank Credit Analyst, a financial research service, said a decline in rates other than those on super-safe
Treasury bonds "would be the key indication that conditions are finally improving. If that does not happen,
then the Fed will be forced to consider more drastic action, including direct purchases of securities."

Stocks Explode in Relief --- Blue Chips Soar 420.41 On Brokers' Earnings; A One-Day Wonder?
Tuesday's Markets
Stocks Explode in Relief --- Blue Chips Soar 420.41 On Brokers' Earnings; A One-Day Wonder?

19 March 2008
The Wall Street Journal

News of stubborn inflation couldn't stop a relief rally in stocks, as earnings reports from Lehman Brothers
Holdings and Goldman Sachs Group provided no unpleasant surprises for investors still rattled by last week's
collapse of Bear Stearns. provided no unpleasant surprises for investors still rattled by last week's collapse of
The market roared higher from the opening bell to send the Dow Jones Industrial Average soaring 420.41
points, or 3.51%, to 12392.66 by day's end. All 30 Dow components finished higher, led by an 11% gain for
Citigroup. Other financials also rose. J.P. Morgan Chase was up nearly 6%.
The rally endured despite some disappointment that the Federal Reserve didn't cut interest rates as much
as some had expected when it announced a three-quarters of a point reduction in its key lending rate in the
afternoon. In fact, stocks extended their surge after a brief push lower on the news failed to gather
momentum.
It was the Dow's biggest point gain since July 2002, although the nearly 417-point gain just a week ago was
slightly greater in percentage terms. That gain, however, was almost entirely reversed by Friday.
"You see these one- or two-day rallies and it fizzles out," said Tim Hayes, chief investment strategist at Ned
Davis Research. The Dow industrials remain 13% below their record close in October and down 6.6% so far
this year.
Until stocks can show evidence of follow-through buying after big rallies, "the best way to play this market is
to give the benefit of the doubt to the downtrend and don't be too quick to try and pick a bottom," Mr. Hayes
said.
The Standard & Poor's 500-stock index rose 4.24%, or 54.14 points, to 1330.74, its biggest percentage gain
since October 2002. The 4.19% gain in the Nasdaq Composite Index was its largest in percentage terms
since March 2003.
The Russell 2000 index of small companies stocks surged 4.83%, with a 31.45 point gain that was the largest
in the index's history.
Still, from their highs in October, the S&P 500 is down 15% and the Nasdaq is off 21%. The Russell is off
20% from its high last July.
A measure of how bad things have gotten lately is that the earnings reports that sparked yesterday's rally
showed profits cut in half at a pair of Wall Street's biggest firms. Lehman posted a 57% drop in its fiscal
first-quarter net, due to losses in its fixed-income business, and Goldman said its fiscal first-quarter profit
dropped 53%, in part due to $2 billion in losses on mortgage and other credit investments.
But both reports were better than expected and provided some relief to Wall Street about the health of both
firms' balance sheets.
Lehman, whose shares fell 19% Monday, rebounded 46%, although they remain down 9% since the end of
February. Goldman Sachs rose 16%. Investors had been nervously awaiting the earnings reports, wondering
if either firm would show signs of the kinds of strains that led to Bear Stearns's downfall.
The rally briefly faltered at midafternoon, reducing the gains, after the Fed's announcement that it was
lowering the target for its key federal-funds rate by three-quarters of a point to 2.25%. Although an aggressive
move by historical standards, it disappointed some in the market who had been betting on a
full-percentage-point cut.

In the bond market, some noted that two Fed officials dissented from the decision and argued for a
less-aggressive rate reduction.
In addition, the Fed said in its announcement that while the outlook for economic activity has weakened,

World News: Vietnam Tries to Cut Loose From Falling Dollar --- Unlinking Currency May Curb Inflation But Irks Exporters
World News: Vietnam Tries to Cut Loose From Falling Dollar --- Unlinking Currency May Curb Inflation
But Irks Exporters
By James Hookway
1,214 words
19 March 2008
The Wall Street Journal
J
A8
English
Copyright 2008 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
HANOI, Vietnam -- Vietnam is trying to unshackle its fast-growing economy from the sliding U.S. dollar, a
tactic that might stem rampant inflation but is rattling exporters and Vietnamese who have been using
greenbacks as a daily currency for years.
Earlier this month, Vietnam decided to partially abandon its de facto dollar peg as part of an effort to curb the
highest inflation rates it has seen since 1995. After considering the move for several weeks, the central
bank on March 10 widened the band in which it allows the Vietnamese dong to rise or fall against the dollar in
one day to 1% from 0.75% previously. The bank said it plans to expand the band to 2%, and the black market
is already pricing the dong much more strongly than the official rate -- a striking reversal of the days when the
dollar commanded a premium on the backstreets of Hanoi and Ho Chi Minh City.
Freeing the dong will let it strengthen against the dollar "and will help tame the higher cost of oil and other
imports," says Vo Tri Thanh, head of the Institute of Economic Planning, a state-run advisory group.
Dollar-based prices of those items are relatively cheaper when translated into a strengthening Vietnamese
currency.
The result for many Vietnamese is a sudden loss of confidence in the dollar, a currency widely used here as a
convenient alternative to the unwieldy dong. The largest Vietnamese note is the rarely seen 500,000 dong
bill. It is worth about $31.
Tellers at branches of Military Bank, among others, have begun turning away visitors trying to exchange
dollars for dong. They say banks worry the dollar's value will fall further. Some exchange counters are
unplugging digital displays showing buy and sell prices for foreign currencies in the hope it will put people off
trying to sell dollars. Ordinary savers are draining dollar accounts to make dong deposits instead, while others
are turning to gold as a safe harbor.
Some of the country's biggest exporters, meanwhile, have written letters to Vietnam's leaders pleading for
help as their dollar-based revenue slumps. Unable to exchange their dollar income for local currency, some
are having to borrow dong to cover their local costs.
Other countries are also allowing their currencies to appreciate against the dollar to tackle inflation. They
include China, which has allowed its currency to rise around 3% against the dollar this year. Kuwait last year
began pegging its dinar to a basket of currencies instead of solely to the dollar. Other Persian Gulf states are
also studying whether to change their dollar pegs.
But few nations have been as dependent on the dollar for their economic progress as Vietnam. The dong's
virtual peg -- a narrow trading band set by the central bank that allowed the dong to rise or fall 0.75% a day
-- helped provide investors with the confidence they needed to put money into a country that only began
opening up to foreign business in the early 1990s.
The popularity of dollars was boosted further by Vietnamese who settled in the U.S. after the Vietnam War
and who regularly sent dollars home to their families.
When the dollar began its decline in 2002, the narrow trading range helped boost Vietnamese textiles and
electronics exports because they were cheaper for U.S. customers than similar products made in Thailand,
Malaysia or the Philippines. This helped Vietnam become a stellar performer in a high-achieving
neighborhood; its economy has grown by an average of 7.5% a year since 2000.
Later, the dong's tendency to follow the dollar contributed to an inflationary bubble as rising prices of oil and
other commodities -- as well as Vietnam's unchecked growth -- began to affect the country's economy.
Inflation hit a 13-year high of 15.7% in February, prompting a series of stiff measures designed to suck
money out of the local banking system.

--- Page 66 ---
Page 66 of 212 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
The most radical response was to widen the band in which the dong is allowed to trade. Since the band was
widened on March 10, the official, central-bank rate for the dollar has fallen a slim 1%, from 16,030 dong to
15,855 dong. But on the black market, where currencies are traded in gold shops and cafes and from which
banks are increasingly taking their lead, the dollar was fetching 15,500 yesterday. That is 3% off the March
10 rate, and an indication that Vietnamese think worse is to come as America's financial problems multiply.
Vietnamese are now trying to get their hands on as much local currency as they can. With the U.S.
apparently falling into recession, banks are reluctant to buy dollars from exporters or individuals.
Vietnam's small stock market, Asia's best performer in 2006 before fading in 2007, has buckled under the
severity of the adjustments now under way. Stocks first slumped when the central bank ordered local
lenders to buy up government bonds in order to drain dong from the financial system and put a cap on
inflation. After the band widened and the dollar began its fall, investors worried that foreigners wouldn't be
able to find enough dong to buy stocks. On some days, there has been hardly any trading.
The government has responded by announcing that its sovereign wealth fund would buy up shares of the
country's leading companies, a measure that some government advisers have privately criticized as distorting
the true value of the market.
The VN Index at the Ho Chi Minh Stock Exchange in Ho Chi Minh City has fallen nearly 50% off its peak of
1170.67 in March last year. Yesterday the index closed at 588.26 points.
With trading volumes dwindling, some stock traders are now venturing into more liquid sidelines. Tran Thi
Hong Lan, a director at VinaSecurities Co. in Hanoi, last week opened a new restaurant in the city with a
partner to tap Vietnam's growing tourism industry, a venture she had planned before the dong was freed from
its dollar peg. "Cash is king," Ms. Lan says, predicting that when the stock market recovers, it will rise along a
more modest trajectory than before.
The Vietnam Association of Shrimp Exporters recently sent a petition to the Minister of Agriculture pleading
that the central bank instruct local banks to convert their dollar earnings into dong.
Phan Van Danh, head of the An Giang province seafood cooperative, told local newspapers that seafood
processors are now buying less from local fish farmers because they can't make enough money exporting
fish. Mr. Danh says 50,000 to 70,000 tons of fish have been left unsold since the dong was unshackled from
the dollar.
Despite its inflation problem, Vietnam is still a cheaper place to do business than many other parts of Asia.
Foreign manufacturers continue to flow into the country, and this could keep upward pressure on prices, say
economists such as Song Seng Wun, who covers the Southeast Asian economy at CIMB-GK Research Pte
Ltd. in Singapore.
---
Nguyen Anh Thu contributed to this article.

Bear Stearns's Ripples Reach Corporate Sector
Bear Stearns's Ripples Reach Corporate Sector
By Timothy Aeppel, Julie Jargon and Erin White
1,084 words
18 March 2008
The Wall Street Journal
J
B1
English
Copyright 2008 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Corporate executives woke up to a gloomier business outlook yesterday morning.
In recent weeks, economic barometers and talk of recession had already made CEOs wary. But the collapse
of financial titan Bear Stearns Cos. has intensified the sense that no business is immune and that the
financial crisis could touch every business, from makers of construction machines to retailers and telephone
companies.
Dyke Messinger, president of Salisbury, N.C.-based Power Curbers Inc., a maker of machines that pour
cement curbs, just got back from a trade show in Las Vegas. The mood there was "cautiously optimistic," he
says, but that was before the collapse of Bear. "When you have a shock like this, the question is whether the
optimism that was still there will go into the tank."
He now fears customers will cut back more than they already have -- his U.S. business is off by about a
quarter -- while the slowdown could now stretch far into 2009. Even before this latest shock, he had cut his
yearly budget for capital expenditures more than 60% to less than $100,000. "We've said we're not going to
spend any money unless it's necessary to keep a machine running," he says.
The problems on Wall Street come at a time when the economy is already flirting with recession. In the latest
signal of underlying weakness, the Federal Reserve reported yesterday that its index of industrial production
dropped 0.5% in February, while the New York Fed's widely watched Empire State manufacturing survey
plunged to a negative 22.23, a low for the index, which measures general business conditions.
For many companies, the mess on Wall Street is just another signal that the U.S. is headed into a prolonged
downturn. That, in turn, feeds a growing sense of caution that could make a sharp recession inevitable. Mark
Bissell, president of Bissell Homecare Inc., a privately held maker of vacuums and other floor-care items in
Grand Rapids, Mich., says his business grew about 10% in the first quarter. During downturns, people tend to
stay home more and try stopgap approaches such as shampooing dirty carpets rather than buying new floor
coverings.
"But given what I see going on today, if there were a big acquisition opportunity for us -- where we'd have to
become leveraged -- I probably wouldn't do it," Mr. Bissell says. "Now is not the time to make risky moves."
The financial crisis has already made it harder to do deals, and it is altering the structure of many of the
buyouts that do occur. Gary Rodkin, chief executive of ConAgra Foods Inc., says the worsening credit crunch
means food companies like his won't be making acquisitions unless they can use cash or stock. That's bad
news at a time when ingredient costs are soaring and margins are being squeezed.
"Getting the financing done, whether on our side or the other, is impossible," he says. "It's crystal clear:
Financing is almost nonexistent."
The lack of credit may be slowing restructuring in such businesses as consumer products, where many
companies are considering shedding operations. For instance, Procter & Gamble Co., which announced this
past August that it was "more likely to be a seller of businesses than a buyer," to date has split off only its
Folgers coffee business. "That process seems to be slower than usual," says SunTrust Robinson Humphrey
analyst Bill Chappell. He blames the recent credit crunch for the sluggishness of other possible P&G
divestitures, such as the Braun small-appliance unit and the pet-food division. "In a normal market there
probably would be plenty of buyers to take those businesses off their hands quickly," Mr. Chappell says. A
spokeswoman for P&G declined to comment.
Michael Kanazawa, chief executive of Dissero Partners, a management consultancy based in Oakland, Calif.,
says that in the past, companies could often sell troubled businesses to private-equity firms, which would then
turn them around. Today, companies will have to turn around their struggling businesses themselves.

--- Page 71 ---
Page 71 of 212 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Kevin Coyne, a management consultant and professor at Emory University's business school, says the credit
crunch is especially hard on small businesses and start-ups, which are seen as riskier bets and typically need
financing to implement their ideas. The financing shortage can have a dampening effect on innovation, he
says. For example, smaller telecom providers, such as wireless Internet start-up Clearwire Corp. and
struggling Internet phone specialist Vonage Holdings Corp., are already having a tougher time tapping the
credit markets, analysts and people close to those companies say.
Meanwhile, the problems are hitting retailers at both ends as customers snap their wallets shut and retailers
attempt to finance their normal operations. Women's clothing retailer Talbots Inc. has struggled in recent
weeks to persuade banks to increase its working-capital lines of credit. As a result of slow sales and the tight
credit markets, the Hingham, Mass., retailer is cutting expenses, opening fewer stores and buying less store
merchandise as it tries to conserve funds. It did strike a deal with one lender on Friday to increase a credit
line.
Of course, some companies are benefiting from the current conditions. For those with plenty of cash, for
instance, now is a good time to buy. As of the end of last month, Illinois Tool Works Inc., Glenview, Ill., had
already completed 13 acquisitions in 2008.
In the energy sector, many larger oil companies have taken advantage of years of historically high prices to
clean up their books. Both Exxon Mobil Corp. and Chevron Corp. have more cash on their balance sheets
than debt. And unlike Wall Street firms, oil companies' value is in hard assets, including petroleum reserves,
pipelines, refineries and drilling rigs.
Many companies are also benefiting from the drop in interest rates. Dan Ariens, CEO of lawnmower maker
Ariens Co., in Brillion, Wis., says he is now paying about a full percentage point less on a loan package
negotiated at the end of last year. At CVS Corp., Chief Financial Officer David Rickard says the interest rate
the Woonsocket, R.I., company pays on $1 billion in commercial paper has dropped two percentage points to
3.5% in the past month, a decrease that translates into millions of dollars of savings on interest payments.

article.

Crude-oil futures closed sharply lower yesterday as fears stemming from the takeover of Bear Stearns Cos.
at a rock-bottom price hit commodities hard.
Meanwhile, gold set a milestone, with the nearby-month contract settling above $1,000 a troy ounce for the
first time. Gold tested the level last week.
Light, sweet crude for April delivery fell $4.53, or 4.1%, to settle at $105.68 a barrel on the New York
Mercantile Exchange. The percentage drop was the largest since Aug. 6, and oil hasn't fallen this much in
dollar terms since January 1991, when the U.S. launched an operation to drive Iraqi troops from Kuwait and
simultaneously opened its strategic crude reserves.
The April crude contract managed to hit $111.80, the eighth straight session where a record was set, in
electronic trading yesterday. But futures quickly declined by more than $6 from those levels. Prices reacted to
an across-the-board selloff in response to J.P. Morgan Chase & Co. agreeing to acquire Bear at a massive
discount.
The deal, along with the Federal Reserve's emergency cut to its discount rate Sunday, provided further
evidence of serious problems in capital markets. Declines in banking-sector stocks led to a wider drop in the
stock market -- causing investors to pull out of other markets, including commodities.
"People liquidate positions because they need money for other things," said Mark Waggoner, president of
Excel Futures. "Sometimes they just bail out of everything."
Silver and copper prices fell, while gold, the traditional safe haven in tough times, shed most of its big early
gains in the broader liquidation trend. Still, Comex gold for March delivery gained $3.20 per troy ounce, or
0.3%, to end at $1,001.40.
In grains, soybeans, corn and wheat all tumbled by the maximum amounts allowed by exchange rules. Sugar
and coffee both fell more than 10%.
"You're seeing massive risk aversion," said Leonard Kaplan, president of Prospector Asset Management.
"Everybody is getting out of everything, in every commodity."
The energy complex was particularly hard-hit, including a 6.9% drop in reformulated-gasoline blendstock, or
RBOB, futures. This is the biggest drop for the front-month contract in its Nymex trading history.
"Today was such carnage . . . sometimes I thought it was just throwing the baby out with the bath water," said
Kevin Mallon at Prebon Energy, who called the decline in RBOB "overdone."
This isn't the first major decline for crude futures since they took off in late January. April crude dropped 2.9%
March 4, only to settle at a high the next day.
Analysts and traders said yesterday's selloff was different, however. "It's been pretty much a one-way market
for a long time, and it can't go on forever," said Tom Bentz, with BNP Paribas. "At some point, you're going to
get corrections."

Fed Passes First Test: Stocks Stabilize
Monday's Markets
Fed Passes First Test: Stocks Stabilize
By Tom Lauricella and Liz Rappaport
722 words
18 March 2008
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2008 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
The nightmare scenario didn't happen.
The effective demise of Bear Stearns, the nation's fifth-largest investment bank, threatened to spread chaos
through financial markets when trading opened. But stocks ended the day mixed after clawing back from
significant early losses, the dollar declined -- but not steeply -- and credit markets showed modest signs of
stability.
Among major indexes, the Dow Jones Industrial Average, which was down more than 190 points at its
intraday low, ended up 21.16 points, or 0.2%, at 11972.25, driven by a gain of 10.3% in the shares of J.P.
Morgan Chase, which scooped up Bear Stearns for $2 a share.
But the Standard & Poor's 500-stock index slipped 0.9%, or 11.54 points, to 1276.70, led by a 3% drop in its
financial components. The tech-focused Nasdaq Composite Index also was off.
Gold futures officially topped $1,000 a troy ounce for the first time, as the nearby-month contract rose $3.20
to $1,001.40 -- the first close above $1,000 of the front-month contract.
The orderliness of trading marked the passage of an important test for the Federal Reserve, which has
undertaken unprecedented steps to keep markets calm in recent days.
"Without the Fed acting as lender of last resort, today could have been much worse," said Mickey Levy, chief
economist at Bank of America.
But sentiment remains deeply wary, and the Fed faces its next test today, when officials meet to consider
how much to cut the benchmark federal-funds rate. The Fed is expected to cut rates by anywhere from half a
percentage point to one percentage point.
Earnings reports from Lehman Brothers Holdings and Goldman Sachs Group are also sure to attract intense
market scrutiny after the collapse of Bear Stearns. Lehman shares lost 19% of their value yesterday -- after
tumbling by nearly 50% at one point -- as investors worried that another big investment bank could hit the
rocks because of financing problems. Morgan Stanley shed 8% and Merrill Lynch fell 5.4%.
The Fed said during the weekend that it would allow certain brokerage firms to borrow from the central bank
on the same terms as commercial banks. In addition, it has said it would provide as much as $30 billion in
financing to backstop the less liquid holdings of Bear Stearns.
The moves were important because securities dealers had been finding private money markets shutting down
on them, because of fears about the value of the collateral they used as short-term loans.
The gain in J.P. Morgan alone provided 30.69 points of the Dow's advance. By mid-afternoon, the pressure
began to lift among non-financial stocks.
Faring particularly well were shares health-care companies, which tend to do relatively well during an
economic downturn, as well as technology stocks, which have taken a beating lately. Dow component
Johnson & Johnson rose 2.2% and Microsoft was up 1.2%.
The Dow industrials are now 16% below their record close in October, while the S&P 500 is 18% below its
own October record.
With expectations for more Fed easing, Treasurys rallied, particularly short-term securities, as investors dove
into the safe-haven of these liquid government securities.

--- Page 75 ---

As prices rose, the yield on the one month T-bill plummeted to 0.51%, from 1.17% on Friday, while the
Treasury's $24 billion auction of three-month T-bills was met with strong demand to yield 1.1%, the lowest

But the dollar fell, trading as low as 95.72 yen during the day, its lowest level since August 1995. The euro hit
a fresh record against the greenback at $1.5747.

iShares S&P Global Financials, was off 2.1%. (ETFs are similar to mutual funds, except that they are listed
on exchanges and trade all day like stocks.)

The Financial Select ETF had only about 0.3% in Bear Stearns as of March 13, he said.
"The key question, of course, is which shoe will be the next to drop amid manifold signs that a massive
deleveraging cycle is heavily stressing the capital markets," Mr. Ptak wrote. "It's possible that we could see
other banks and very large hedge funds fail, though the magnitude of any fallout is difficult to gauge."
KBW Capital Markets ETF, which invests in broker-dealers and other financial stocks, lost nearly 7%.

ProShares, rose nearly 4% and is up 35% over the previous three months as a result of the credit turmoil and
banking write-downs.
Conversely, an ETF that makes a leveraged bet on financials, Ultra Financial ProShares, was down nearly
4%. International and emerging-markets stocks also felt the pain of the global selloff, including iShares MSCI
Emerging Markets and iShares MSCI EAFE, an ETF following an index of international developed-markets
stocks.
In bonds, iShares Lehman 20+ Treasury Bond was up nearly 1% as investors bought up safer bonds and
pushed yields lower. Another haven, StreetTracks Gold Shares, rose about 0.5% as precious metals, which
are seen as a hedge against inflation and disaster, continued their rally.
Weakness in the dollar pushed the CurrencyShares Euro Trust, which tracks the movement of the greenback
versus the euro, up by 0.5%.

Dollar Bears
Dollar Bears
By Reuven Brenner
1,098 words
18 March 2008
The Wall Street Journal
J
A23
English
Copyright 2008 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
How can $20 billion in Bear Stearns market value evaporate overnight? Though many are asking this
question today, few are noticing the fact that, since 2002, trillions of dollars worth of business and
U.S.-government debt value has evaporated. This happened because the Federal Reserve has neglected the
dollar.
There is much talk now about restoring "trust" in U.S. financial markets. You cannot restore trust while
signaling that no steps will be taken to prevent the further fall of the greenback. Capital, be it to shore up
financial institutions, or buy up much-devalued U.S. assets -- in terms of some currencies, U.S. real estate
has plunged by more than 40% -- will stay on the sidelines as long as the Federal Reserve and the
government do not take action to fix monetary policy.
In a Financial Times op-ed the other day, Alan Greenspan says that a measure of stability will be restored
when house prices stabilize, which may be accurate. But why would capital flow into real estate denominated
in dollars that are still expected to plunge?
The view expressed on this page by former Federal Reserve Board member Robert McTeer -- that the Fed
now must give priority to the liquidity crisis and neglect the dollar -- is inaccurate, too. The liquidity crisis and
the stable dollar are related. The vast extension of credit since 2002 could have never happened if the Fed
had sustained a stable value for the dollar. And by stable, I do not refer to focusing on short-term "core
inflation," which has been leaving out food and energy prices and mismeasuring much else.
There were many signals suggesting that the Fed has been mismanaging the dollar, which, with the
mismanagement of the yen and the uncertainty of the unique euro (the first paper currency not anchored in
either gold or backed by a government), became the sole reserve currency in the late 1990s. When one
manages a reserve currency, it needs an "outside" anchor to keep global credit expansion under control. The
Fed has not provided one, which it could have done by keeping the dollar stable compared to gold and other
commodities.
There are many "moneys" in circulation -- a few of them domestic and others foreign. We have $100, $10 and
$5 bills. We also have quarters, dimes and pennies, and an array of foreign currencies circulating. They all
function as a medium of exchange here and around the world. The U.S. denominations stay in fixed relation
one to the other, whereas the others have been fluctuating by 50% or more relative to one another.
Ask then: Why can the $10 bill always be exchanged for 40 quarters, whereas it varies daily in terms of how
many foreign units it can be exchanged for? After all, the demand for the $10 bills and quarters fluctuates.
With fewer parking meters, more automatic machines accepting credit cards and more cell phones, the
demand for quarters has been dropping, and the demand for paper bills rising. Why doesn't the value of the
quarters drop relative to the $10 bill? The answer is that as fewer coins are being used, they get returned to
the central bank, which perhaps sells them as scrap metal, issuing $10 dollar bills instead.
This same process can work for any two currencies of two different countries. If people decide that they
intend to spend less in the U.S., they show up at a bank or brokerage house and ask to exchange their
unwanted U.S. dollars for, say, Canadian ones. The Fed notices that the demand for the U.S. dollar
decreased, so it absorbs the unwanted U.S. dollars by selling U.S. denominated bonds on the open market
(or selling Canadian dollars or Canadian dollar-denominated bonds).
The exchange rate between the two countries stays stable -- pretty much as the "exchange rate" between the
two U.S. denominations stays stable. Technically, the value of the Canadian dollar to the U.S. dollar can be
as easily fixed as the value of the one U.S. denomination relative to another.
But why is it important to keep exchange rates between two countries stable to start with?

--- Page 78 ---
Page 78 of 212 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Whereas we can have as many mediums of exchange as we want, we must price goods and services, and
get into longer term contractual agreements, to ensure stable capital flows. But should we price in euros or in
U.S. dollars? Or, as has been done for centuries, in terms of gold?
What matters is to have an agreed-upon unit of account in terms of which trading partners could price every
contractual agreement, preferably having the medium of exchange serve as the unit of account, too. This
anchor is missing in the international monetary system today, and is one reason for the financial havoc
surrounding us. The volatility around a downward trend of the U.S. dollar -- which was closest to playing the
unit of account/exchange double role for half a century -- has diminished its efficiency for these uses. As a
result, contracts are shorter. Companies pay substantial insurance fees (and derivative contracts are in the
trillions) to stay in their line of business and not get into exchange-rate trouble. Resource-rich countries keep
their resources in the ground longer, rather than selling them for papers with uncertain values. And some
investors have been loosing substantial amounts by holding bonds of devaluing currencies. Now people are
deciding not to invest in U.S. dollar-denominated assets.
The issue isn't that "we will never have a perfect model of risk," as Mr. Greenspan appears to think. What we
need is accountability, not perfection. With the proper anchor, central banks can sustain a stable value for
their currency, and that is what they must be held accountable for. If they do that, even if financial institutions
experiment with a wide range of innovations they cannot expand credit too much.
Crises bring us back to the basics. There is prosperity when talent is matched with capital and everyone can
measure in a predictable unit of account. Stable currencies bring about long-lasting, wealth-creating
employment. The destruction of wealth by debasing currencies occasionally "creates" employment (with
people being forced to accept lower-paying second and third jobs) but it signals poverty, not prosperity.
---
Mr. Brenner is partner at Match Strategic Partners and a professor of management at McGill University. He is
author of "Force of Finance" (Thomson, 2002) and co-author of the forthcoming "A World of Chance"
(Cambridge University Press).

Page 79 of 212 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
CME Moves Closer to a Deal for Nymex
Deals & Deal Makers
CME Moves Closer to a Deal for Nymex
By Aaron Lucchetti
816 words
17 March 2008
The Wall Street Journal
J
C6
English
Copyright 2008 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Chicago Mercantile Exchange operator CME Group Inc. moved closer to a pact to buy Nymex Holdings Inc.,
a $9.3 billion deal that expands the CME into energy trading while underscoring the importance of technology
to exchanges.
The deal, set to be announced as soon as this morning, would end more than 135 years of independence for
the New York Mercantile Exchange, Nymex's oil and gas market. Boards from both companies met yesterday
to vote on the transaction.
The CME purchase would mark the latest union between two exchanges once split by products and
geography, but now pushed together by market forces and customer demands for lower costs. Nymex
shareholders are expected to receive cash and CME shares worth about $100 each, valuing the company at
$9.3 billion.
The combined exchange will have offices in both Chicago and New York, uniting CME's financial and
agricultural products with Nymex's energy and metals contracts. Nymex's energy business would stay in
Manhattan for at least five years -- an important detail to gain support of New York politicians such as Sen.
Charles Schumer -- though not necessarily at its current location in the Financial District.
Nymex's business has boomed as investors bet on the soaring prices of oil and gold, two of Nymex's largest
contracts. In 2007, Nymex traded 25 million gold-futures contracts, up 57% from the previous year, and 121
million crude-oil futures, a 71% jump that made it the busiest commodity futures contract globally, according
to the Futures Industry Association trade group.
On the strength of those contracts as well as growth in natural gas, silver and copper trading, Nymex sold
shares to the public in late 2006. But exchange analysts never viewed Nymex as a likely survivor in the
exchange merger game because it lacked the core trading technology that puts buyers and sellers together.
Instead, Nymex signed an agreement to use CME's technology, which also powers CME's trading in its
bellwether contracts based on stock indexes and short-term interest rates.
Last year, CME bought the owner of the Chicago Board of Trade, the bond and grain exchange that
previously used a European company for the technology to match buyers and sellers.
As exchanges grow larger, the companies that own them are finding it increasingly important to own the
technology used to match buyers and sellers. Last year, NYSE Euronext said it would buy the remaining
stake it didn't own in AEMS, a financial- technology vendor it used for much of its trading.
NYSE and Nasdaq OMX Group Inc., with the latter's recent purchase of exchange and technology company
OMX AB, are both hoping to sell more financial technology to brokerage firms and other exchanges in coming
years. Merging with OMX gives Nasdaq more "ability to deliver sophisticated exchange technology to both
developed and emerging markets," Bob Greifeld, Nasdaq's chief executive, said recently.
In the derivatives business, where hedge funds and other sophisticated investors make high-stakes bets with
borrowed money, exchanges that didn't own technology often have sold to technology owners. Even the
electronic options market, International Securities Exchange Holdings Inc., outsourced much of its technology
to OMX. Last year, ISE sold to Eurex, a large European derivatives exchange that develops its own
technology.
Another options and stock market, the American Stock Exchange, has disclosed plans to sell to NYSE
Euronext, after years of being known for innovative products but lackluster trading technology.

--- Page 80 ---
Page 80 of 212 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Like many exchanges, Nymex had relied on open-outcry trading, where jacket-clad traders gathered in a pit
to set market prices based on frantic buying and selling. With the advent of electronic trading, more
customers demanded bypassing the pits to cross trades in cyberspace. When Nymex delayed adopting
electronics, Wall Street customers launched rival energy exchange IntercontinentalExchange Inc., which
remains Nymex's most formidable competitor.
The Chicago exchanges -- CME and the Chicago Board of Trade -- faced similar electronic threats from
European exchanges and others who wanted to take away their financial futures business. But the two
exchanges were able to fight off competition by embracing electronics and have become the dominant
traders of exchange-traded derivatives.
That dominance could become an issue as the CME and Nymex attempt to gain antitrust and other regulatory
approval to close their deals. The Justice Department recently wrote a letter raising the idea of whether
futures exchanges should open up their lucrative clearing divisions to more competition, essentially making it
look more like the cutthroat stock and stock-options trading businesses in the U.S.
CME officials have fought that idea, saying most futures exchanges overseas have the same business model.
But in this deal, regulators must come to terms with approving a company that would, upon closing, have the
vast majority of U.S. futures trading.

Page 81 of 212 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Natural Gas Rides High, for Now --- Prices May Ease As New Supplies Reach the Market
Leading the News
Natural Gas Rides High, for Now --- Prices May Ease As New Supplies Reach the Market
By Ben Casselman
682 words
17 March 2008
The Wall Street Journal
J
A4
English
Copyright 2008 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Natural-gas prices are near two-year highs and outpacing price increases for oil, driving up costs for
companies and consumers.
But many market watchers predict a fall in coming months, as new supplies come to market following years of
flat production.
The price of gas hit $9.868 per million British thermal units Friday after topping $10 earlier last week, as
investors spooked by the stock markets and worries about the U.S. economy poured money into commodities
like oil and gold. Gas is up 32% since the start of the year, putting it near its highest level since hurricanes
Katrina and Rita hit the gas-rich Gulf of Mexico nearly three years ago.
Gas meets about a fifth of the nation's electricity needs, heats about half of its homes and is an important
ingredient for making plastics and fertilizer. The fuel is increasingly in demand because it burns more cleanly
than coal.
Analysts cite a variety of factors for gas's run-up in addition to the general rise in commodities. They include a
colder-than-expected winter; falling production in Canada; and rising demand for oil, which has been
propelled by investors seeking a safe haven.
"I don't think we would have hit $10 [per million BTUs] if crude oil wasn't up at $100 [a barrel]," says Guy
Gleichmann, president of United Strategic Investors Group in Hollywood, Fla., which doesn't have an
investment in gas.
The rise comes at a time when oil-and-gas companies, citing long-term trends, are redoubling efforts to find
new gas supplies. Driving the trend: concerns over pollution and climate change. The U.S. Natural Gas
Council, an industry group, predicts a carbon-capping system such as one now being considered by
Congress could lead to a 20% increase in natural gas consumption over the next decade.
"We clearly see that people are rejecting coal plants in every state we can think of, so we've got to come up
with alternatives that work for the American consumer," says James Hackett, chairman and chief executive of
Anadarko Petroleum Corp., one of the nation's largest producers of natural gas. "The power market will
increasingly turn to natural gas as its one alternative."
Those efforts are adding new gas supplies to the market after a decade of flat or falling production.
Production last year rose 4.3% according to one government measure, marking the largest rise in more than
20 years. Most expect production to continue to rise this year.
Over the short term, that could lead to new supplies that drive down prices later this year. Summer pricing
predictions vary widely, but most analysts say natural-gas storage figures released last week would support
summer prices around $7 to $8 per million BTUs.
"At some point these high production rates are going to push prices down," Credit Suisse analyst Jonathan
Wolff says.
J. Marshall Adkins, an analyst with Raymond James in Houston, predicts prices could drop to $5 per million
BTUs or lower over the summer, especially if warm weather reduces demand in coming weeks.
Many wells being drilled today require gas prices above $7 per million BTUs to be viable, and exploration in
emerging areas such as the Marcellus shale in Pennsylvania could be even more expensive.
Some market watchers expect prices to remain high in coming months. Natural gas producers may have
trouble keeping up last year's growth rate, they say. Offshore production in the Gulf of Mexico shot up 7% in

--- Page 82 ---
Page 82 of 212 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
December from the previous year, but that growth was almost entirely due to Independence Hub, a massive
new offshore drilling project that went online late last year. With no similar projects anticipated in the
immediate future, most analysts expect offshore growth to level off this year or next.
Meanwhile, onshore production growth has relied heavily on the increased production in Texas and
Wyoming, which together accounted for 78% of onshore growth last year.
