The Bernanke Reflation
REVIEW & OUTLOOK (Editorial)
The Bernanke Reflation
828 words
29 February 2008
The Wall Street Journal
J
A16
English
Copyright 2008 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
For readers under age 30 who are wondering why they are suddenly paying $3.15 for gasoline and $2 for
milk, the answer is that this is what an inflation looks like. Those of us of a certain age remember it well, if
painfully, and judging by the noises coming from the Federal Reserve of late we had all better get used to it
again.
First, Fed Vice Chairman Don Kohn declared that, while inflation was worrisome, the Fed now views
recession as the more urgent danger to fight. Then on Wednesday, Fed Chairman Ben Bernanke told
Congress that the Fed will do whatever it takes to stop the credit squeeze from becoming a recession. That's
about as close as a central banker will get to saying that he's thrown price stability to the wind. If inflation
rises -- as it now surely will -- then the Fed will worry about that later, after the economy is safely past the
credit crunch.
Right on cue, the best indicators of inflation expectations hit new highs. Oil has surged past the once
astronomical $100 mark and is now $102 a barrel; as recently as September, it was $70. Gold is nearly $975
an ounce, and the $1,000 threshold seems inevitable. The euro has broken $1.50 for the first time, while
commodity prices in general are hitting record highs. These increases will roll through the rest of the economy
and lift prices for food, energy, and countless other goods and services.
Call it the Bernanke reflation, though it's more precise to call it the Fed's second inflation gamble of the
decade. The first was Alan Greenspan's roll of the dice from 2003-2005, keeping interest rates too low for far
too long in the aftermath of the dot-com bust. That spurred the first boom in commodity prices, as well as the
subsidy for debt that led to the housing bubble and the credit mania whose collapse we are now dealing with.
Mr. Bernanke was a Fed Governor during much of that time, and he seems to have learned his lessons all
too well. He's now going all-in for round two.
Naturally, the Fed and its most vocal constituencies -- Wall Street and politicians -- see nothing much to
worry about. Wall Street sees a reflation as a way to ease its credit problems, as price increases ease debt
burdens and perhaps reflate housing values. Congress and the White House see a way to perhaps avoid a
near-term recession, which might get them past the election.
As for the Fed, its Governors are dusting off their favorite intellectual justifications. We are told that inflation
isn't as bad as it seems because "core inflation" -- which excludes food and energy prices -- isn't rising as
fast as the consumer price index. However, food and energy are what most Americans are having to spend
ever more of their paycheck to buy. Thus the Bernanke reflation is in part self-refuting even as a short-term
recession antidote, because it robs consumers of some of their discretionary income just when the economy
needs it.
Meanwhile, even the Phillips Curve is making a comeback. That's the notion -- popular before it was
discredited in the 1970s -- that there is a trade-off between inflation and economic growth. In its new version,
argued by Fed Governor Frederic Mishkin, the Phillips Curve doesn't exist in the long term but does in the
short term. Thus the Fed can afford to open the monetary flood gates now because the slower economy
could lead to lower prices later this year. Then when the economy recovers, the Fed can afford to tighten
money again.
This is a beguiling intellectual construct, but it puts a great deal of weight on Fed Governors to know when to
tighten again. They were supposed to do something similar in 2003-2005, but they were terribly wrong. Then
as now they were also dismissing such forward-looking price signals as gold and oil and instead focusing on
such misleading indicators as "core inflation" and the money supply. Mr. Mishkin may be seen as a
monetary wizard at the Fed, but to investors around the world he is beginning to look more like a high-class
inflationist.

--- Page 147 ---
Page 147 of 212 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
The people who aren't being fooled by all this are the American people. They don't pay their bills with "core"
dollar bills, and they know those dollars buy less with each passing month. This explains their rising economic
anxiety -- and anger -- better than trade or job losses do, especially since the job market has remained
relatively healthy. Inflation is the great thief of the middle class, as even Americans who don't recall the
1970s are learning. With its all-in reflation bet, the Bernanke Fed is gambling with their money.
(See related letters: "Letters to the Editor: Fighting Recession Instead of Inflation" -- WSJ March 7, 2008)

Downbeat reports about the economy sent the dollar to a record low against the euro -- even as stocks
shrugged off the bad news, and a rally in crude oil past $100 a barrel, to close higher.
The dollar's weakness came on new signs of distress in the housing market and a higher-than-expected
increase in wholesale inflation.
"The dollar is clearly feeling the brunt of a pessimistic view on the U.S. economy," said John McCarthy,
manager of currency trading at ING Financial Markets. One euro briefly fetched more than $1.50, a record,
late yesterday, after trading at $1.4966 as of the 4 p.m. close for stocks.

Machines, which announced a stock-buyback plan valued at as much as to $15 billion and raised its
earnings-per-share forecast accordingly.

fuel a technology rally. Intel rose 3.8%, and Microsoft rose 1.9%.
The tech-stock-heavy Nasdaq Composite Index was up 0.8% to 2344.99.
The gloomy U.S. economic news contrasted with Europe, where German business confidence showed an
unexpected rise earlier in the day. The divergence helped shake the dollar out of a trading range against the
euro that has been in place since late November.
Behind the currency movements are the vastly different approaches adopted by the U.S. and European
central banks over an uncertain economic outlook. European policy makers have remained focused on risks
from inflation and have held key interest rates steady. The Federal Reserve has slashed interest rates in a
bid to stimulate economic growth and seems ready to do so again.
That has increased pressure on the dollar, because lower interest rates make short-term dollar deposits less
attractive. The dollar's fate will depend partly on whether investors believe Europe will share in U.S. economic
ills -- and whether its central bank, too, will be compelled to cut interest rates. Some economists say they
believe the European Central Bank might moderate its economic growth forecasts as soon as next month.
Even as IBM's news attracted buyers to the stock market, investors overlooked several other pessimistic
developments, including a surge that took crude-oil prices back above the psychologically important
$100-a-barrel level. Crude-oil futures gained $1.65, or 1.7%, to an exchange-record $100.88 a barrel in New
York amid speculative buying and supply concerns sparked by renewed unrest in Nigeria and Iraq.
Although the market shrugged off crude's rise, most companies might suffer if the rally lasts. Consumers'
spending power might be sapped as they pay more at the pump, diverting money from purchases that
generate most companies' profits.
Another source of anxiety for U.S. consumers: falling home prices. The S&P/Case Schiller home-price index
showed home prices fell 8.9% in fourth quarter of 2007.
Analysts said the stock rally fits the pattern of the last few sessions. The Dow has now rallied for three
straight trading days, leaping 3.3%, or more than 400 points, amid a steady stream of mixed news. It is down
4.4% this year.

Ahead of the Tape
Ahead of the Tape
Ahead of the Tape
By Scott Patterson

Fannie, Freddie
May Show
It's 'Prime' Time
Forget subprime.
As the housing market continues to fall apart, more woes are hitting mortgage loans to homeowners with
solid credit records. That has troubling implications for Fannie Mae and Freddie Mac, which purchase and
guarantee a wide variety of home mortgages, mostly prime.
Fannie is slated to report financial results today, Freddie tomorrow. Analysts will be looking for signs of
problems in their more-credit-worthy loan portfolios. As of December, 2.2% of all prime mortgages were at
least 60 days past due, up from 1.5% a year earlier, and the highest level since 1998, according to First
American CoreLogic. More than one-fifth of subprime loans were 60 days or more past due. A check of
delinquency rates for large, high-quality mortgages shows that "credit quality is now deteriorating sharply
even for prime mortgages," Morgan Stanley analyst Kenneth Posner wrote in a recent research note
reiterating a negative outlook on Fannie. Delinquency and foreclosure rates for jumbo loans in December
were more than double the previous January.
Friedman Billings Ramsey analysts expect delinquencies on Fannie Mae's single-family credit book to rise as
high as 1.5% in the next few months from the current 0.9%, already well above historic levels.
One growing problem: More homeowners have an incentive to default as property values drop, and they owe
more on their mortgages than the home is worth. Yesterday the S&P/Case-Shiller index showed that home
prices in 10 major metropolitan areas fell 8.9% in the fourth quarter from the previous year, the biggest drop
in the index's 20-year history.
The decline in home prices leaves $1.75 trillion in mortgage debt outstanding under water, according to
Moody's Economy.com. With roughly $1 trillion in subprime mortgages outstanding, that means a large slice
of prime loans, as well as Alt-A -- a category between prime and subprime -- is mired in what economists call
"negative equity."
It adds up to a big negative for homeowners -- and for Fannie and Freddie.
Bernanke's Unenviable Spot:
Inflation, Recession, the Hill
The camera lights on Capitol Hill may feel especially hot to Ben Bernanke today.
The Federal Reserve chairman will deliver his semiannual testimony on monetary policy to the House
Financial Services Committee just as almost everything under his direct purview seems to be moving in the
wrong direction. Despite his interest-rate cuts, the economy is slowing and long-term fixed-mortgage rates
are rising.
Meanwhile, inflation fears are bubbling. Crude oil, gold and wheat are at or near record highs. The dollar is
at a record low against the euro. The Labor Department yesterday reported the biggest 12-month surge in
producer prices since October 1981. And 10-year Treasury inflation-protected securities yesterday priced in
a 2.46% annual increase in the consumer-price index over the next 10 years, up from about 2.2% a month

--- Page 153 ---
Page 153 of 212 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
ago and near the highest levels of 2007, according to Tony Crescenzi, chief bond-market strategist at Miller
Tabak.
Yesterday, Fed Vice Chairman Donald Kohn dismissed inflation concerns and said the Fed will fight
recession by any means necessary. But central bankers will now have to think long and hard before cutting
rates aggressively again.

State-Run Funds See Gold --- As Profits Increase, Investors Pour Cash Into Commodities
Commodities Report
State-Run Funds See Gold --- As Profits Increase, Investors Pour Cash Into Commodities
By Spencer Swartz
614 words
27 February 2008
The Wall Street Journal
J
C8
English
Copyright 2008 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
LONDON -- With juicy profits from rising exports of raw materials, government-run funds in Asia and the
Middle East are now investing billions of dollars directly into crude oil, copper and other commodities in a
development that could reshape markets globally.
Total commodity investments by what are known as sovereign-wealth funds are around 1% of the funds' total
assets of nearly $3 trillion -- an average based on estimates by Morgan Stanley, Global Insight and other
analysts. That amounts to a sizable $30 billion worth of state funds in commodities, up from a fraction of that
amount five years ago.
But state-run fund investments in energy, agricultural and other commodity markets are expected to get even
bigger due to rapid economic growth in China, Saudi Arabia and other emerging markets and related high
commodity prices.
Sovereign-wealth fund commodity investments, which are also aimed at diversifying funds' investments, are
expanding trends started by U.S. and European private-equity groups, secretive hedge funds and even
pension funds, which have piled into commodities the past few years amid the run-up in raw-material prices.
A commodity favorite among a number of state-run funds has been gold, which investors have traditionally
used to defend against rising inflation and a weaker U.S. dollar.
Middle East funds have a particular appetite for gold because of rising prices and the declining value of the
dollar, to which many Gulf countries peg their currencies. Gold futures yesterday rose $8.50 per troy ounce,
or 0.9%, to $946.10 on the Comex division of the New York Mercantile Exchange -- matching the recent
Comex record, hit on Feb. 21.
Total sovereign-wealth fund assets are forecast to quadruple to around $12 trillion by 2015, making the funds
the biggest grouping of investors in the world, according to U.S. investment bank Morgan Stanley.
"We've seen more and more investments by sovereign-wealth funds into commodities, like gold, and we're
still at an early stage," says Boris Shrayer, head of investor coverage and strategy for commodities at Morgan
Stanley.
But the financial muscle of state-run investment funds may pose new challenges for market participants and
regulators because it opens the prospect of state funds moving prices in futures markets like corn or copper,
which are much smaller relative to more capitalized markets like crude oil.
Similar to many individual and corporate investors, sovereign-wealth funds are mainly investing in
commodities through exchange-traded funds, which offer investors exposure to a commodity like gold or
wheat without accepting the delivery of products.
Commodity investments can also be a double-edged sword for state-run funds. Some countries, like China,
are big commodity producers and consumers and also fork out a lot of cash to import large quantities of
products such as coal and copper. Being too aggressive with their investments could contribute to higher
prices for a given commodity and push up a country's import bill.
In other trading yesterday:
WHEAT: Futures closed mostly higher, as the markets continue to focus on short supplies of spring wheat,
traded at the Minneapolis Grain Exchange. March MGE wheat fell $1.60 to $22.40 a bushel, while
most-active May wheat gained $1.35 to close at $18.4325.

--- Page 155 ---
Page 155 of 212 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
CRUDE OIL: Futures soared to a new high of $100.88 on the Nymex yesterday, up $1.65, amid continued
weakness in the U.S. dollar and growing concerns about inflation. Yesterday's surge in oil prices was also
supported by concerns that tension in Nigeria and Iraq could disrupt oil supplies.

Inflation May Be Worse Than We Think
Inflation May Be Worse Than We Think
By David Ranson
925 words
27 February 2008
The Wall Street Journal
J
A17
English
Copyright 2008 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Amid Wall Street's recession panic, the latest official inflation news has received less attention than usual.
The "headline" consumer price index (CPI) for the year ending in January was up 4.3%, the third consecutive
monthly reading above 4%. This has been easy to attribute to rising energy costs, for which the blame is
often misplaced on foreigners and oil companies. But as I've argued on this page in the past, the real problem
is the sick dollar.
The "core" CPI that excludes energy and food shows a 12-month rise of 2.5%. Though higher than the
Federal Reserve's so-called comfort zone, that's not yet dramatic enough to dominate the headlines -- or to
hold the Fed back from responding to the pain that its primary constituency, the banking community, is going
through. Reinforced by intense pressure from Congress, the banking panic has compelled the Fed to take its
eye off inflation. Rates have been reduced by more than two percentage points, and more cuts are
expected.
While the practice of excluding energy and food costs to glimpse the "underlying" rate of inflation meets a lot
of skepticism, the official consumer-price picture still keeps everyone guessing. The root of the confusion is
the fact that the prices consumers actually pay change far more quickly than the CPI.
The CPI utilizes what is, in effect, a form of historical cost accounting. Its shelter component, which carries
the greatest weight, is designed to reflect the average monthly cost to households who moved into their living
space at a wide range of times dating many years back in the past. Other components are based on price
"menus" published by retailers or fees set by public authorities, neither of which changes frequently. In the
financial markets, only prices from recent transactions would provide timely information. But government
agencies are comfortable with cheaper and backward-looking data.
The most timely figures come from the commodity markets, where prices are transparent and reflect
conditions in the immediate present. Yet commentators tend to discount volatile data like energy and food
prices when they assess the "underlying" rate of inflation. This is a big mistake.
Markets look forward, while government surveys of the cost of living are a rearview mirror. A little "indicator
analysis" shows that commodity prices, far from reverting quickly back to the mean, are early-warning
indicators of the future CPI. Last year's large increases in energy and food imply that consumer-price
inflation is going to be much closer to today's "headline" rate of 4.3% than the "core" rate of 2.5%.
Why does this matter? The accompanying graph shows how rapidly the purchasing power of income declines
from an ongoing inflation of 4%. (see accompanying illustration -- WSJ February 27, 2008) After nine years,
an income of $100,000 is worth only $70,000. After 17 years its purchasing power has been cut in half, and
after 30 years by about 70%.
The cumulative loss of purchasing power if inflation persists above 4% is an awesome prospect that is surely
going to be unacceptable. When the Fed gets its eye back on the inflation ball, a return to higher interest
rates will not be far behind.
But worse may be yet to come. While commodities like energy and food are leading indicators of the CPI,
precious metals like gold are, in turn, leading indicators of energy and food. It's sobering to note that
precious-metals prices this year are running more than 30% ahead of where they were a year ago. The
situation evokes one of Johnny Cash's songs, "Five Feet High and Rising."
Historically, CPI inflation is more closely related to prior changes in the price of gold than most people
realize. The correlation is obscured by great differences in short-term behavior -- the price of gold is
notoriously volatile, while the CPI is very slow-moving. It's therefore necessary to take account of gold-price
changes over a multi-year time frame.

--- Page 157 ---
Page 157 of 212 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
There is a remarkable parallel between annual CPI inflation and the cumulative change in the price of gold
measured from eight years before. A similar graph could be plotted for silver, and the parallel can also be
seen in cross-section by comparing countries over time with varying degrees of currency instability.
Historical CPI data in the U.S. are complicated by occasional changes in the methodology the government
uses to calculate the index. In the late 1990s, one of these changes reduced the reported inflation rate
significantly. But taken as a whole, the relationship suggests my following rule of thumb to estimate CPI
inflation at any time: Divide the percentage change in the gold price from eight years in the past by 80, and
add three. This rule of thumb has largely worked over the past several decades. In the last eight years the
price of gold has risen 225%. The rule therefore comes out with an answer that puts inflation a lot closer to
6% than 4%.
Commodity prices provide what may be the most pertinent information for anticipating how high CPI inflation
is going to get. Even if the gold boom suddenly ends, which I doubt it will, 4% inflation looks very much on
the low side. Higher ground, anyone?
---
Mr. Ranson is head of research at H. C. Wainwright & Co. Economics.
(See related letters: "Letters to the Editor: Inflation Rears Its Ugly Head Once Again" -- WSJ March 4, 2008)

Credit Markets
Treasurys Turn Back After Morning Rally
By Min Zeng

23 February 2008
The Wall Street Journal

B2
English
Copyright 2008 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Treasurys were hammered in late-session trading as U.S. stocks turned around and following a hawkish
warning about inflation risks from Federal Reserve Bank of Dallas President Richard Fisher.
The selloff Friday accelerated after CNBC reported that a bailout of financial guarantor Ambac Financial
Group Inc. could be announced early next week, easing some concern about subprime-mortgage turmoil that
has haunted the markets since last summer.
As investors dumped government debt to get into equities, the two- and 10-year sectors ended the week on a
down note, giving up a rally in the morning session.
Mr. Fisher, in an interview with Bloomberg News, said he is hearing increasing expressions of concern about
inflation from executives, which has "gotten my attention." He also said the U.S. probably will see slower
economic growth rather than a deeper downturn.
Mr. Fisher, who is a voting member of the policy-making Federal Open Market Committee, voted against the
Fed's half-percentage-point interest-rate cut Jan. 30 due to concerns about inflation and a belief the Fed's
prior actions would be sufficient to get the economy back on its feet.
Worries over inflation have moved to the front burner after a higher-than-expected consumer price index
Wednesday, and as crude oil and gold set records. A weak dollar also added to the worries as it pushed up
import prices.
"Fisher really hit the market's nerve. His comments play into people's fear of inflation," said T.J. Marta,
fixed-income strategist at RBC Capital Markets in New York. Inflation eats into the returns on fixed-income
securities. "The Fed is basically fighting a two-front war with one weapon, and that isn't a good recipe."
Treasurys had benefited earlier as U.S. stocks initially tumbled amid worries about recession risks. That
helped bonds extend their rally Thursday after the Federal Reserve Bank of Philadelphia's manufacturing
gauge signaled the economy may be contracting.
Speculation that more financial guarantors may be downgraded also added to the buying in Treasurys,
traders said. Downgrades on credit ratings of these insurers will lead to downgrades of the corporate and
municipal bonds they guarantee, which may lead to a chain reaction in the broader credit markets.
"The subprime situation is far from over. It is like a big dark cloud hanging over the markets," said Bill
Strazzullo, chief market strategist at financial advisory firm Bell Curve Trading in Freehold, N.J.
The 10-year Treasury note Friday fell 2/32 point, or 62.5 cents per $1,000 face value, at 97 20/32. Its yield
rose to 3.790% from 3.782% Thursday, as yields move inversely to prices. The 30-year bond was down
16/32 point at 96 20/32 to yield 4.583%, up from 4.552%.
CIFG Put on Watch
Moody's Investors Service Friday put small financial guarantor CIFG Guaranty on watch for a possible
downgrade because of its exposure to risky mortgages and uncertainty about the company's direction.
The review of the top-notch triple-A rating will be resolved in two weeks, Moody's said. Four financial
guarantors already have been stripped of their triple-A ratings by at least one of the three major rating firms,
and others are on review for downgrades, as securities they insure that are related to subprime mortgages
continue to lose value.

Earnings Digest: Sale of Mine Stake May Strengthen Rio's Hand
Earnings Digest: Sale of Mine Stake May Strengthen Rio's Hand
By Andrew Edwards
588 words
22 February 2008
The Wall Street Journal
J
A10
English
Copyright 2008 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Rio Tinto PLC said it would sell its stake in a U.S. gold mine for $1.7 billion, in a deal that could help the giant
mining conglomerate make its case that a takeover offer it faces from BHP Billiton is too low.
Barrick Gold paid well above the $600 million or so that analysts had expected Rio Tinto's 40% stake in its
Cortez Hills, Nev., operation would fetch. The outcome bodes well for Rio's efforts to dispose of a total of $10
billion of assets in 2008, and may bolster the company's case that its assets are undervalued. Rio has twice
rejected buyout offers from BHP, the latest of 3.4 BHP shares for each Rio share.
The news came on a day that included a mixed bag of earnings reports from gold producers and another
jump in gold prices. Barrick and another miner, Goldcorp, reported higher fourth-quarter net income, the
latter aided by a prior-year write-down, but Newmont Mining Corp. swung to a loss on a $1.12 billion
write-down of its reserves.
Barrick reported net income of $537 million, or 61 cents a share, up from $418 million, or 48 cents a share, a
year earlier. Revenue grew 42% to $1.92 billion.
Barrick's gold production fell 12% as costs per ounce rose 31%. Copper production rose 1%, while costs slid
1%. The Toronto-based company's realized price increased 28% for gold and 2.2% for copper.
The company expects 2008 gold production of 7.6 million to 8.1 million ounces, versus 2007's 8.06 million
ounces and copper production of between 380 million pounds and 400 million pounds, down from last year's
402 million pounds. Costs are expected to be higher than 2007 owing to "higher energy costs," among other
reasons.
Newmont, the world's second-largest gold miner behind Barrick, reported a fourth-quarter net loss of $289
million, or 63 cents a share, compared with year-earlier net income of $223 million, or 49 cents a share.
The company said the $1.12 billion write-down was the result of a "new industry-developed" interpretation of
impairment analysis of "recent reserve-replacement results." Results also included a $900 million gain on the
sale of its merchant-banking unit and other assets. Revenue slipped 1% to $1.41 billion.
Newmont's gold sales fell. Copper sales were cut nearly in half to $121 million. The Denver-based
company's cost of gold production rose 19%, while realized prices rose 29%.
Canadian miner Goldcorp said net income hit US$256.6 million, or 36 cents a share, up from $66 million, or
11 cents a share, a year earlier. That period included a $174.7 million write-down on mining interests.
Revenue jumped 42% to $679.8 million.
Production rose 9% to a record 638,900 ounces of gold. The company's costs increased 22% to $195 an
ounce, primarily from the strength of the Canadian dollar.
Lightly traded but nearby February gold settled at $946.10 per troy ounce on the Comex division of the New
York Mercantile Exchange, up $11.50, or 1.2%, amid worries about inflation and even possible "stagflation."
The metal closed at its 12th record of the year. It has gained 13% so far this year. The most-active April
contract settled at $949.20 up $11.40, or 1.2%.

Gold, Platinum, Copper Shine --- Stagflation Worry Helps Drive Gains; Behind Java's Jump
Commodities Report
Gold, Platinum, Copper Shine --- Stagflation Worry Helps Drive Gains; Behind Java's Jump
By Allen Sykora and Matt Whittaker
597 words
22 February 2008
The Wall Street Journal
J
C7
English
Copyright 2008 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Precious and base metals soared, with gold and platinum touching records and copper hitting a 19-month
peak on the weakness of the dollar and a host of other factors.
Precious metals were also lifted by worries about inflation and continuing credit-market jitters. Platinum
remained underpinned by power-supply shortages in South Africa that are reducing mining output at a time
when demand is strong.
"You've got pretty good fund buying in gold, silver, platinum and palladium," said George Gero, vice
president with RBC Capital Markets Global Futures.
Nearby February gold gained $11.50 to $946.10 an ounce on the Comex division of the New York Mercantile
Exchange and reached a peak of $953.40. That is a spot-month record.
Oil futures topped $100 a barrel this week before retreating yesterday, reinforcing worries about inflation, Mr.
Gero said. That supports gold because investors often buy it as a hedge against rising prices.
Several analysts cited fears of possible "stagflation," the combination of inflation and stagnant consumer
demand that occurred in the 1970s and helped send gold to the record highs in 1980 that stood until last
month.
"We're going to continue to see the Fed reducing rates because they are going to try to prevent the economy
from going into recession, if we're not already in one," said David Beahm, vice president with Blanchard & Co.
This comes as recent inflation data have been rising, along with commodity prices.
While it is "a little premature to say whether we're experiencing stagflation," Mr. Beahm said, investors are
"looking for something to grab onto" and thus are turning to precious metals.
Also, further rate cuts could portend more dollar weakness that helps gold, Mr. Beahm said. Market
participants often buy the metal as a hedge against dollar weakness. Furthermore, a weaker U.S. currency
contributes to inflation, Mr. Gero said.
April platinum rose $49.40 to $2,188.20 an ounce and hit a Nymex record high $2,194.80.
Meanwhile, copper was lifted by the soft dollar, declining inventories and ideas that emerging markets could
provide more demand than previously thought.
Nearby Comex February copper rose 10.5 cents a pound to $3.8105 a pound, peaking at $3.8125, the
highest front-month price since July 2006.
Inventories in London Metal Exchange warehouses, watched as a gauge of supply tightness, fell to 135,375
metric tons from 200,975 metric tons Jan. 8.
Eric Wittenauer, an analyst with A.G. Edwards, sees growing emerging-market demand as a trend that will
continue.
"I think that generally speaking that's where the majority of demand -- and particularly demand growth -- lies,
in developing nations, particularly China," Mr. Wittenauer said.
In other markets:
COFFEE: Futures rose, supported by speculative buying and strength in London's robusta market, which
touched 11-year highs. Speculative buying and limited selling by Vietnamese coffee producers -- the world's

Page 162 of 212 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
largest producers of robusta coffee -- have lifted prices recently. The ICE Futures U.S. March arabica coffee
contract gained 1.75 cents to $1.5635 a pound.
CRUDE OIL: Futures settled lower for the first time since Feb. 12 after the release of U.S. data showing an
increase in crude-oil and gasoline inventories last week pulled oil back from $100. On its first day trading as
the front-month contract, light, sweet crude for April delivery settled $1.47, or 1.5%, lower at $98.23 on the
Nymex.

Dow Industrials Tumble 142.96 Points
Thursday's Markets
Dow Industrials Tumble 142.96 Points
By Peter A. McKay
547 words
22 February 2008
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2008 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
A sharp drop in oil prices hammered U.S. energy stocks and failed to provide the usual boost for other
sectors, as a dismal reading on factory activity from the Federal Reserve's Philadelphia branch renewed
investors' worries about recession.
The Dow Jones Industrial Average ended the day down 142.96 points, or 1.2%, at 12284.30, down 7.4% so
far this year. Exxon Mobil was off 1.3%, and Chevron fell 1.8%.
The blue-chip average was up more than 75 points early in the day. The swoon began when the Philadelphia
Fed said its business activity index, a measure of regional manufacturing, skidded to its lowest reading since
2001, below analysts' expectations. The report returned attention to the duration and severity of a possible
recession in the U.S.
"Most folks who were in denial about our being in a recession in the first place have now come over to the
dark side," said Jon Najarian, an independent trader and founder of OptionMonster.com, a Chicago research
firm. "It's just a question of what kind of recession we'll have."
Like many Wall Street pros, Mr. Najarian believes the U.S. economy will experience a relatively mild
downturn this year, lasting two or three quarters, when the stock market may be able to hold steady.
But some are looking for a more dire outcome. In a research note, Merrill Lynch economist David Rosenberg
warned that the Philadelphia Fed data signaled the economy could be headed into a deeper recession, akin
to the one it suffered in 1990, which continued into the following year.
Meanwhile, inflation remained a concern. Gold, benefiting from its historic role as an alternative store of
financial value when confidence is waning in paper currency, soared to its 12th record this year on the Comex
division of the New York Mercantile Exchange, up $11.50 a troy ounce to $946.10. Several analysts cited
fears of possible "stagflation," the combination of inflation and recession that occurred in the 1970s.
Other commodities also gained as rising recession fears hurt the dollar. Because most commodities trade in
dollars, a weaker dollar drives up prices. Silver rose to its highest level since December 1990, up 21% this
year. The Dow Jones-AIG Commodity Index was up 0.6%, or 1.281 points, at a record 206.687.
But crude-oil futures, after fueling inflation worries with record prices topping $100 a barrel, fell $1.47 to
$98.23 a barrel on the Nymex. Traders said oil was hurt by a weekly rise in U.S. inventories and investors'
scramble to roll their bets to April futures from the recently expired March contracts. Oil is still ahead 2.3% so
far this year.
The Standard & Poor's 500-stock index was off 1.3%, or 17.50 points, at 1342.53, led by a decline of nearly
3% in its energy sector. The S&P is off 8.6% this year.
As investors sought safe havens, prices for Treasury soared. The 30-year bond leapt 1 14/32, or $14.38 per
$1,000 invested, lowering its yield to 4.552%. The two-year note gained 8/32 to yield 1.993%.

Crude Sticks Above $100 On Speculators' Activity
Commodities Report
Crude Sticks Above $100 On Speculators' Activity
By Gregory Meyer
450 words
21 February 2008
The Wall Street Journal
J
C4
English
Copyright 2008 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Crude-oil futures reached a record high for the second straight session, remaining above $100 a barrel.
Trading was volatile ahead of yesterday's expiration of the front-month contract for benchmark crude on the
New York Mercantile Exchange.
Light, sweet crude for March delivery settled up 73 cents at $100.74 a barrel, while the more-heavily traded
April Nymex contract was unchanged at $99.70 a barrel.
After a two-week-long run-up fueled largely by supply concerns, prices rose as speculative money flooded the
market.
"The funds are buying into this," said Mark Waggoner, president of Excel Futures, a commodities broker in
Huntington Beach, Calif. With the expiration of the March contract, "the market becomes extremely volatile,"
he added.
Helping propel prices was the release of Federal Reserve minutes revealing concern about economic risks.
The dollar fell on the news.
"The Fed minutes certainly implied more rate cuts, which could lead to further deterioration of the dollar and,
obviously, increased value for crude contracts," said Mike Fitzpatrick, an analyst at brokerage MF Global in
New York.
While the April contract ended unchanged, it clawed back from earlier lows, suggesting crude's recent price
strength may endure. March crude hit a record intraday high of $101.32 a barrel, which "will now provide an
upside target to April values," said Jim Ritterbusch, president of trading-advisory firm Ritterbusch and
Associates in Galena, Ill.
Yet crude's strength in the absence of new developments suggesting a tighter supply and demand balance
led some to question the rally's longevity.
In a weekly report, MasterCard Advisors LLC, a division of MasterCard Inc., revealed data showing that U.S.
gasoline demand in the week ended Feb. 15 was 4.4% below a year ago, the fourth consecutive year-to-year
decline and the biggest since the end of December.
Analysts also expect U.S. oil stockpiles to have grown for the sixth straight week in government data due out
today, which could weigh on oil prices.
The market's willingness to keep oil aloft could complicate matters for the Organization of Petroleum
Exporting Countries when producers meet March 5.
In other commodity markets:
SILVER: Strength in crude oil and gold lifted silver prices to a life-of-contract high of $17.79 an ounce. March
silver on the Comex division of Nymex rose 25.2 cents to settle at $17.76.
ORANGE JUICE: Prices rose, rebounding after a recent sell-off, after frozen concentrated orange-juice
futures found support at key technical-chart points. ICE Futures U.S. FCOJ March futures gained 2.40 cents
to $1.2465 a pound.

Dow, up 90.04, Shrugs Off Inflation
Wednesday's Markets
Dow, up 90.04, Shrugs Off Inflation
By Tom Lauricella and Peter A. McKay
745 words
21 February 2008
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2008 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Stocks recovered from early losses to end the day higher, as the market brushed aside reports of stubborn
inflation pressures that may complicate the Federal Reserve's ability to cut interest rates.
Down more than 100 points during the day, the Dow Jones Industrial Average ended 90.04 points higher, up
0.7%, to 12427.26. The blue-chip average is down 6.3% this year.
The session began with sobering news as the Labor Department reported its consumer-price index rose by a
larger-than-expected 0.4% in January, after a similar increase in December. In addition, crude-oil prices hit
records, settling 73 cents a barrel higher at $100.74 on the New York Mercantile Exchange. That is a 5% gain
so far this year and 68% higher than a year ago.
Led by oil, raw-materials prices continued their broad-based rise. The Dow Jones-AIG Commodity Index,
which ended at a record Tuesday, rose 0.1%, or 0.152 point, to 205.406, its second straight record. Gold
prices also rose to a high, closing at $934.60, up $8.00 a troy ounce, or 0.9%.
"For a while, people were so consumed by the credit crunch that it was easy to dismiss oil as a minor
speculative bubble. Now people are paying more attention," said J.P. Morgan Chase economist James
Glassman.
Higher commodities prices have helped the stocks of many raw-materials companies in recent months. That
was the case again yesterday, when the Dow Jones basic material index rose 1.34%. That index is up 16.5%
from a low hit on January 22.
The Standard & Poor's 500 gained 0.8%, or 11.25 points, to 1360.03, down 7.4% in 2008. The
technology-focused Nasdaq Composite Index rose 0.9%, or 20.90 points, to 2327.10. The Nasdaq is down
12% year-to-date.
The inflation news prompted futures traders to scale back expectations for additional Fed rate reductions.
Before the consumer-price data, a half-point cut in the key federal funds rate at the Fed's next policy-making
meeting on March 18 had been viewed as a certainty. And traders had priced in an 80% chance for another
quarter-point cut in April. By day's end, futures contracts suggested a 92% chance for the half-point cut in
March and 60% odds for another easing in April.
The rally took hold after the Fed released the minutes of its late-January meeting, which described Fed
officials as concerned with "downside risks" to the economy even after their recent aggressive interest cuts.
That bolstered Wall Street's confidence that the central bank will cut its key interest-rate target next month to
boost U.S. economic growth, despite the more cautious stance in the market for futures tied to interest-rate
predictions. Interest-rate cuts tend to help corporate profits and boost stocks.
"The Fed has told us that it's all about growth and they are going to do whatever it takes to make sure growth
is underpinned . . . even if we come out of this with an inflation problem," said T.J. Marta, fixed income
strategist at RBC Capital Markets.
Tech stocks, meanwhile, were helped by a strong earnings report from Dow component Hewlett Packard,
which late Tuesday reported a 38% gain in fiscal first-quarter profit and forecast better earnings for the
current quarter than analysts were predicting. Hewlett Packard shares ended 7.9% higher.
Small-company stocks, typically seen as the most sensitive to the outlook for the U.S. economy, were
especially strong. The Russell 2000 index of small-cap stocks gained 7.68 points, or 1.1%, to 710.02. It's now
up 5.7% from its lowest close this year on Jan. 22.

--- Page 167 ---
Page 167 of 212 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
One factor that may be working in favor of stocks is that investors' flight to safety in Treasury securities
appears to be abating. "People are saying, 'Let's face it, yields are really low'" and putting their money to work
elsewhere, said Jim Caron, interest-rate strategist at Morgan Stanley. Prices for Treasurys fell as investors
went into stocks, sending yields higher. The yield on the 10-year Treasury note rose to 3.913%, its highest
since Dec. 31 and up from a four-and-a-half year low of 3.425% on Jan. 23.

Rally Killer: Crude at $100 Weighs on Dow --- Blue Chips, Oil Swap Triple-Digit Figures; Consumer
Stocks Suffer
By Peter A. McKay
729 words
20 February 2008
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2008 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Crude oil surged to a record of more than $100 a barrel, quashing a daylong stock rally as investors sold
consumer stocks that may suffer as Americans spend more at the pump.
Other raw materials also rallied, and mixed profit news from two retail bellwethers further muddied the outlook
for consumer spending, which represents more than two-thirds of U.S. economic output.
The Dow Jones Industrial Average was up nearly 160 points early in the session and maintained a 100-point
gain well into the afternoon. It ended down 10.99 points, or 0.1%, at 12337.22 and is off 7% this year.
Dow component Wal-Mart pointed to cautious consumer spending and said it expects earnings this quarter to
come in at less than expectations. Still, the retailer's stock rose 0.4% after the company posted a 4% climb in
fiscal fourth-quarter earnings.
OfficeMax surged 5.1% after the office-supplies retailer reported a 23% rise in fiscal fourth-quarter net
income. But sales fell, and the company said it expects economic weakness to hurt future results.
The Standard & Poor's 500-stock index fell 0.1%, or 1.21 points, to 1348.78, hurt by a 0.6% pullback in its
consumer-discretionary sector. The S&P is off 8.1% so far this year.
Investors in financial companies were sorting out the effects of Wall Street's credit bets. Credit Suisse
reduced the value of certain asset-backed securities by $2.85 billion, blaming some of the hit on
"mismarkings and pricing errors" by traders. The firm's American depositary shares fell 5.2%.
Marc Pado, U.S. strategist for brokerage Cantor Fitzgerald, said he doesn't expect the broader stock market
to be able to sustain a rally until perhaps April, when first-quarter reports from financial firms likely will provide
more details about the value of complicated credit bets on their books.
Chevron and Bank of America made their debuts as Dow components. Higher oil prices helped Chevron gain
1.5%; Bank of America slipped 0.07%.
Hewlett-Packard rose 5% in after-hours trading; it reported a 38% gain in fiscal first-quarter earnings after the
close.
Concerns about supply fueled the big rise in energy prices. Traders bid up refined fuels after an explosion
Monday at Alon USA's refinery in Big Spring, Texas. Comments by Iran's oil minister spurred worries that the
Organization of Petroleum Exporting Countries may cut production at its March 5 meeting. And rebels
threatened oil facilities in Nigeria.
Crude-oil futures jumped $4.51, or 4.7%, to settle at $100.01 a barrel on the New York Mercantile Exchange.
Oil is up 4.2% on the year. The price set an intraday record of $100.10 a barrel.
Commodity prices have generally strengthened in recent weeks in part because of confidence that demand
can remain solid in the U.S. But if demand shows signs of slipping, or if high costs begin to translate into
higher inflation readings in the months ahead, the stock market could suffer.
"This was a pretty nasty day," said strategist Al Goldman at brokerage A.G. Edwards & Sons in St. Louis. "It
looks to me like the market is going to trend lower," perhaps until the fall, when the effects of Federal Reserve
rate cuts and Washington's fiscal stimulus plan may finally produce greater U.S. growth.

Stocks in Shanghai rose, shrugging off news that Chinese inflation had risen to its highest level in more than

The Shanghai Composite Index, which tracks yuan-denominated Class A shares and foreign-currency Class
B shares, rose 2.1% to 4664.29. The index is still down more than 11% this year.
Analysts were divided on the likely extent of the central bank's policy response to data showing consumer

said Howard Gorges, vice chairman at South China Brokerages in Hong Kong. "But they also realize that the
government can't control everything, because there are shortages of material that will keep inflation high for
the time being."
"If inflation in China looks like [it is] escalating, the government will take more macro measures, like
interest-rate hikes and bank-reserve requirements being increased," he said. "[But] they don't want to tighten
up too much and kill business."
Stephen Green, Standard Chartered Bank's China head of research, wrote that China faces "a serious
short-term inflationary threat," saying he expected four more rate increases as well as more increases in the
reserves banks are required to hold at the central bank.

Index rallied 2.3% to 13942.24, encouraged by gains in Shanghai.
Bank of China surged 7.8% on news of its subprime provisions and search for a stake in a life insurer this

structured-credit trading business by $2.85 billion after an internal review. Credit Suisse said it suspended

further write-downs to reveal. The French CAC 40 index rose 0.5% to 4885.83
Barclays jumped 3.7% in LONDON as the bank's 3.4% drop in 2007 net profit largely matched market
expectations. A relatively small increase in write-downs and tight cost controls helped to balance a weaker

Politics & Economics: Party Mate's Run Shows Waning Mugabe Support --- Electoral Challenge To Zimbabwe Leader Emboldens Rivals
Politics & Economics: Party Mate's Run Shows Waning Mugabe Support --- Electoral Challenge To
Zimbabwe Leader Emboldens Rivals
By Sarah Childress
926 words
19 February 2008
The Wall Street Journal
J
A10
English
Copyright 2008 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
A surprise electoral challenge by a respected member of Zimbabwean President Robert Mugabe's party has
rattled the political opposition and galvanized many who have long sought to unseat Mr. Mugabe, who has
ruled for 28 years.
On Friday, Simba Makoni registered as an opposition candidate in the March 29 presidential polls. Given a
short campaign period and the country's history of vote-rigging and intimidation, few expect victory for Mr.
Makoni or for longtime opposition leader Morgan Tsvangirai, who also registered Friday to contest the
election. But Mr. Makoni's break from Mr. Mugabe's tightly controlled ranks indicates that the president's grip
on power may be weakening.
Since announcing he would run late last month, Mr. Makoni has been unceremoniously expelled from the
ruling party and insulted and threatened by the president's supporters. He has also attracted some support
from at least one corner of Zimbabwe's established but fragmented opposition party, the Movement for
Democratic Change, which is headed by Mr. Tsvangirai.
Mr. Makoni said he has the support of several members of the ruling party, the Zimbabwe African National
Unity-Patriotic Front, or Zanu-PF. He hasn't released a list, but some prominent party members who would be
expected to publicly defend Mr. Mugabe haven't done so. That may be a sign that other party stalwarts are
wavering, says Sydney Masamvu, a Zimbabwean and senior analyst based in South Africa for the
International Crisis Group.
"You're seeing a creation of a cross-party fight against Mugabe," Mr. Masamvu says. "Mugabe is being forced
from within [Zanu-PF] and outside, and Makoni is a representation of that fight."
Mr. Mugabe has ruled from within a tight circle of supporters who remain loyal because of his patronage. But
the loss of their support, which would include elements of the army and intelligence sector, would make it
more difficult to crush the opposition or manipulate the vote, strategies that international observers say he
has employed in the past.
Zimbabwe's polls next month will be the continent's next big electoral test, coming in the wake of badly
marred elections in Kenya, which led to weeks of violence and a political stalemate that hasn't been resolved.
Nigeria's judiciary is reviewing the country's presidential vote last spring, which was riddled with violence and
irregularities and condemned by international observers as deeply flawed.
And late last year, South Africa muddled through a tense ruling-party battle that saw the country's president
lose out to a onetime ally as party leader.
Mr. Makoni spent the years of Zimbabwe's revolutionary struggle studying chemistry in the U.K., and returned
to serve in the new government in 1980. Mr. Mugabe had come to power after defeating white supremacist
rule in what was then known as Rhodesia.
Bright and well-spoken, Mr. Makoni rose to become an influential member of the Zanu-PF politburo. Younger
than most party stalwarts -- Mr. Makoni is now 57 -- he has generally been considered a moderate. He served
for a time as the executive secretary for the Southern African Development Community, a group that
coordinates economic development in the region.
In the past, Mr. Makoni has broken from the party line in criticizing government policies, including Mr.
Mugabe's contentious policy of seizing commercial farms, a move that devastated Zimbabwe's agricultural
sector. When he was appointed finance minister in 2000, Mr. Makoni recommended the government devalue
the currency to curb rising inflation. He was promptly fired.

--- Page 173 ---
Page 173 of 212 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
After Mr. Makoni announced he would contest the election, the government expelled him from the ruling
party. He will run as an independent.
In recent years, Mr. Mugabe has faced only one major opponent: the Movement for Democratic Change.
Formed by Mr. Tsvangirai, a former mine worker, trade unionist and human-rights activist, the MDC enjoys
more support in the liberal cities than in rural areas, where Mr. Mugabe is still popular. But the party has
never won an election.
International observers have blasted previous polls, citing widespread intimidation, voter fraud and bribery. In
the past, people received state-subsidized maize only if they presented a Zanu-PF registration card. Mr.
Mugabe's electoral commission has maintained that he has been fairly elected.
But the opposition's biggest challenge this year may not even be Mr. Mugabe. By failing so far to rally around
a single candidate, analysts say politicians risk splitting the opposition vote.
Already, Mr. Tsvangirai's MDC apparatus has turned its guns on Mr. Makoni. The media wing of the party has
emailed editorials slamming Mr. Makoni as a political opportunist. At the same time, the leader of a faction
within the party has moved to support Mr. Makoni.
Zimbabwe has vast platinum and gold reserves, a skilled work force, and fertile soil. Yet under Mr. Mugabe,
the economy has crumbled. Inflation has soared above 66,000%, according to the Zimbabwe government.
About 80% of the population is unemployed, and at least three million Zimbabweans, many of them
well-educated, middle-class citizens, have fled to South Africa and other neighboring countries, where they
work menial jobs.
Mr. Mugabe has blamed his country's economic crisis on Western saboteurs hoping to return the country to
white rule. Despite Mr. Mugabe's ruinous policies, many Zimbabweans and other Africans have been
reluctant to criticize the president because of their respect for him as a revolutionary hero.

Exxon Has Off Year Discovering New Oil
Exxon Has Off Year Discovering New Oil
By Jeffrey Ball and Russell Gold
567 words
16 February 2008
The Wall Street Journal
J
A2
English
Copyright 2008 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Exxon Mobil Corp.'s success in finding new oil and natural gas is slipping.
The world's biggest publicly traded oil company by market capitalization said Friday that it replaced just 76%
of the oil and gas it produced last year, using a reserves-accounting method favored by the Securities and
Exchange Commission. Using a different accounting method that Exxon says is more representative of its
business, the company said it replaced 101% of its production last year.
Either way, Exxon was less successful in 2007 than in prior years in finding new fossil fuel. Exxon said its
101% reserve-replacement number in 2007 was the company's lowest in 14 years.
Alan Jeffers, an Exxon spokesman, said the company isn't worried that its 101% reserve-replacement
number in 2007 was the lowest since 1993. Last year was Exxon's "14th consecutive year of more than
replacing what we produced," he noted. "We're proud of that fact."
The lackluster reserve-replacement numbers are a symptom of the difficult operating environment Western oil
companies face. Much of the remaining oil, Exxon Mobil Senior Vice President Mark Albers said this week, is
"found in complex geologic formations, in remote locations and under harsh conditions."
What's more, rising resource nationalism is making Exxon's job more difficult. A big part of the reason Exxon
had such a low reserve-replacement number was Venezuela's appropriation of Exxon assets there. Excluding
the Venezuelan appropriations, and also excluding the smaller effect of property sales, Exxon said its
reserve-replacement ratio in 2007 was 132%.
The announcement also stokes fears about whether there will be enough oil in coming years. Demand is
rising quickly, and the big Western oil companies are not raising production reliably or replacing production
with new reserves. This fear is one reason for the run-up in oil prices in the past few years. New York oil
futures Friday finished at $95.50 a barrel, up four cents for the day and 7.4% below an inflation-adjusted
high of $103.10 a barrel reached in April 1980.
Don't count Exxon out too quickly, says Michelle Michot Foss, head of the Center for Energy Economics at
the University of Texas. The Irving, Texas, company is still meeting its obligations to deliver fuel and return
heady profits. "Even though their reserves figures may be low, there are no signs that they aren't delivering to
their customers," she says.
Reserves accounting by oil companies traditionally has been a matter of interpretation. Oil companies have
calculated their "proved" reserves -- oil and gas that can be produced using standard technology and market
prices -- based on their own internal price assessments. But in the wake of a 2004 reserves-accounting
scandal at Royal Dutch Shell PLC, many oil companies began reporting their reserves according to a method
preferred by the SEC. Under that method, designed to give investors an apples-to-apples comparison among
oil companies, companies calculate their reserves based on commodity prices on a single day: Dec. 31.
Exxon now reports its annual reserve-replacement numbers both according to the SEC's guidance and
according to the company's prior practice. In releasing its numbers Friday, Exxon reiterated that calculating
reserves based on a single day's price is "not relevant to investment decisions."

nearly 5%. Shares of giant retailer Wal-Mart Stores dipped 1%
Economic concerns pushed Treasury prices mostly higher Friday, and the yield on the benchmark 10-year
note sank to 3.78%. But yields were still higher for the week after a selloff sparked by mortgage-bond
investors trying to balance their portfolios on Thursday.

Security and the Falling Dollar
Security and the Falling Dollar
By Judy Shelton
1,313 words
15 February 2008
The Wall Street Journal
J
A15
English
Copyright 2008 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Every year, the Senate Select Committee on Intelligence is briefed by the chief of U.S. intelligence on
potential threats to the nation. The list is sobering, but usually predictable and typically includes global
terrorism, nuclear proliferation and regional conflicts.
But this year, there was a surprising potential foe: the falling dollar. In his report to Congress last week,
Director of National Intelligence Michael McConnell went beyond the conventional world of spycraft. Mr.
McConnell specifically acknowledged "concerns about the financial capabilities of Russia, China, and OPEC
countries and the potential use of their market access to exert financial leverage to achieve political ends." He
noted, in particular, the impact a weak dollar can have on national security: "As the dollar has weakened this
year, some oil producers -- such as Syria, Iran, and Libya -- have asked to be paid in currencies other than
the dollar while others -- such as Kuwait -- are delinking their currency pegs to the dollar."
It's not every day a former Navy vice admiral steeped in the culture of the defense intelligence community
talks like a central banker, but Mr. McConnell clearly recognizes a threat: "Continued concerns about dollar
depreciation could tempt other major producers to follow suit."
The rest of Washington -- and every presidential contender -- needs to start paying attention to the declining
dollar before it develops into a full-blown currency crisis with damaging geopolitical consequences. What
happens if major oil-producing nations decide to abandon the dollar for measuring the value of their most
important export? What if they set up their own monetary union to serve as an alternative to a currency that is
no longer a reliable store of value?
At a time when the Pentagon is focusing on the importance of rebuilding war-torn nations -- restoring civil and
economic stability -- as a prerequisite for lasting peace, we should be emphasizing the confidence-building
aspects of sound money. The dollar's primary role in the world financial system is the most vital nonmilitary
instrument of our national power. We cannot afford to neglect the dollar and thereby give up the global
influence that comes from providing the world's key reserve currency.
It's a matter of global energy security, first and foremost. Russia makes no secret of its ambitions to elevate
the ruble to world reserve currency status. President Vladimir Putin, in a major televised speech last Friday
outlining his strategic goals for the next 12 years, stated that Russia must become "one of the world's
financial centers" -- a logical step, he asserted, given that Russia has accumulated $484 billion in gold and
foreign currency reserves. Mr. Putin earlier called for denominating transactions for Russian oil and gas
exports in rubles. When his chosen successor, Dmitry Medvedev, is inaugurated in May, we can expect a
tightening of the link between Russian energy deliveries and currency requirements imposed on recipient
countries.
A perfect storm for dollar desertion may already be brewing. In the months ahead, China is expected to
export fewer consumer goods to the U.S. with a forcibly-appreciated yuan. Meanwhile, Chinese spending for
Russian oil and gas will likely start to ramp up. Mr. Medvedev, whose duties include serving as chairman of
Gazprom, observed this past summer that the U.S. dollar was not immune to crisis of a "comprehensive,
global character." He was thinking ahead about potential opportunities. "A situation may arise where we,
China, and some other Asian countries will speak of the emergence of a regional reserve currency." The yuan
was a possibility, Mr. Medvedev conceded. "But it is in our interest that it be the ruble."
If gold and foreign currency reserves were the only prerequisite for harboring global ambitions in the
monetary arena, China could make impressive claims of its own with $1.7 trillion as of December 2007, the
world's highest level. Japan comes in second at $973 billion. If you add together the gold and foreign
currency reserves of EU member states that have adopted the euro, including those of the European Central
Bank, the total amount in the EU is $511 billion.

--- Page 179 ---
Page 179 of 212 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Russia trails not so far behind. And beyond its considerable official reserves (which increased by 57% last
year alone) and its alarming energy clout in European and Asian markets, Russia has a growing military
presence. Not that Russia's military capabilities were ever truly contained. But those capabilities have been
constrained as Russia struggled to emerge from its Communist past and join the family of democratic nations.
Political transformation and economic renewal took precedence, so it seemed, over the large demands on
funds that clinging to military superpower status would have required.
Building up a menacing force of strategic bombers, nuclear submarines and new intercontinental ballistic
missiles is a financial luxury -- one that Mr. Putin now seems eager to indulge as Russia fills its coffers. It may
be that he sees it as the essential appendage to global power needed to carry out his strategic vision.
How else to explain the incursion of a Russian warplane on Saturday into Japanese air space, a violation that
caused 22 Japanese fighter jets to scramble and elicited a strong protest to Moscow from the Japanese
foreign ministry? The TU-95 "Bear" bomber is equipped to carry cruise missiles that can deliver a nuclear
warhead.
Japanese newspapers speculate that the intrusion might have been prompted by an annual rally held two
days before in Tokyo demanding the return of four islands seized by Russia in the closing days of World War
II. Both Japan and Russia are strongly motivated to settle the long-standing dispute; Japan wants greater
access to Russian energy supplies while Russia seeks Japanese financial capital to develop its far eastern
regions.
Just last week -- only three days before the Russian warplane sortie -- Japanese Prime Minister Yasuo
Fukuda announced he had received a letter from the Russian president offering to hold talks to resolve the
dispute over the islands. Mr. Fukuda has made plans to visit Moscow in late April or early May, convinced
that working out a settlement with Mr. Putin "is essential to lift Russo-Japanese ties to higher levels." If he
waits until May 9, Mr. Fukuda can take in Mr. Medvedev's inaugural ceremonies as well as the planned
full-scale, Soviet-style military parade -- the first display of weapons on Red Square since 1990.
Not that the crude maneuvering of carrot-and-stick incentives isn't effective, but it is dangerous. Especially
when the dealmaker has shown a high propensity to rely on the stick.
A weakening U.S. currency plays directly into fears about a weakening U.S. economy and gives credence to
self-serving pronouncements about America's weakening role in the world arena. The dollar won't be
strengthened by further interest-rate cuts or more fiscal stimulation leading to inflationary consumer spending.
If the U.S. is to reclaim its position as provider of the world's most trusted currency, we must think more
boldly.
It's time to confront currency disorder. Our goal should be to put forward a new proposal for international
monetary relations on the scale of the 1944 Bretton Woods agreement, invoking the same sentiments that
inspired architects John Maynard Keynes and Harry Dexter White to provide a foundation of hope for a world
all too prone to violence. A global system based on a universally-accepted monetary asset -- the U.S. has the
world's highest level of official gold reserves, followed by Germany and France -- would not only counter
Russia's offensive. It would convert a national security threat into a golden opportunity.
---
Ms. Shelton, an economist, is author of "The Coming Soviet Crash" (Free Press, 1989).
(See related letter: "Letters to the Editor: The Dollar is a Big Element of U.S. Security" -- WSJ Feb. 29, 2008)

Incentive Is Treatment For Health Insurance Costs
Letters to the Editor
Incentive Is Treatment For Health Insurance Costs
213 words
14 February 2008
The Wall Street Journal
J
A15
English
Copyright 2008 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Your editorial "Equity and Health Care" (Feb. 4) correctly notes that President Bush's health insurance
proposal would both decrease the regressivity of the current subsidy and remove the bias favoring
employer-provided health insurance. However, you overlook an important aspect of the proposal.
Since Mr. Bush's health insurance deduction is a flat $15,000 per year regardless of the price of a medical
insurance plan, all people (not just those with "gold-plated" policies costing more than $15,000 per year) will
have an incentive to bargain for less expensive insurance. People will discover that bargaining for a policy
that costs, say, $1,000 less will result in $1,000 of savings that can be spent on other goods and services.
Unlike the existing system, the amount of the health subsidy does not increase for any spending beyond the
first dollar. Choosing a $1,000 less expensive insurance policy in the existing system leads to significantly
less than $1,000 to spend on other things. The Bush plan is a commendable step toward reducing medical
inflation by motivating consumers to bargain for less expensive medical insurance and services.
E. Frank Stephenson
Chairman
Department of Economics
Berry College
Mount Berry, Ga.

Politics & Economics: High Food Prices Stir Movement on Tariffs
Politics & Economics: High Food Prices Stir Movement on Tariffs
By John W. Miller
1,117 words
12 February 2008
The Wall Street Journal
J
A4
English
Copyright 2008 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
BRUSSELS -- The world's scramble for affordable food is tearing at the patchwork of agricultural tariffs that
governments have long used to control trade -- and offering a glimmer of hope to those trying to kick-start a
stalled global trade deal.
Some countries are slashing import duties to attract staples like wheat, rice and cooking oil. Europe,
traditionally the world's most outspoken advocate of protected food markets, recently removed cereal-import
duties for the first time. Others -- notably China -- are raising export duties to keep domestic markets well
stocked.
So far, the situation hasn't forced a rethinking of subsidies that farmers in the developed world receive. But
some say that is an inevitable consequence of higher global food prices. "The market situation now means
there's less pressure on farmers," says Peter Mandelson, the European Union's trade commissioner.
Mr. Mandelson and other top trade officials, including U.S. Trade Representative Susan Schwab, recently
agreed to meet in Geneva before June. It will be their first summit at the World Trade Organization's
headquarters in nearly two years.
The WTO's 151 members have been negotiating a new global treaty since 2001. The so-called Doha round of
talks aims to give developing countries full access to Western food markets in exchange for allowing
wealthier countries to sell services like banking, insurance and construction more easily across the globe.
Talks have foundered over the refusal of the EU and U.S. to cut their lavish farm subsidies.
With today's high food prices, however, "there's much less of a need for protectionism than when we started
in 2001," said Mr. Mandelson.
A global trade deal remains a long shot. The U.S. Congress is currently drafting a farm bill that doesn't
envision cuts in subsidies. And France remains a bulwark of protectionism. "We'll need to see a long period of
high prices before we reduce subsidies," Michel Barnier, the French agriculture minister, said in an interview.
In the meantime, nations are doing what they have to do in order to stay fed.
Last month, China's Ministry of Finance announced it was setting export tariffs on 57 food commodities in the
range of 5% to 25%. The decision sparked concern about China's future exports. U.S. corn futures quickly
leapt to an 11-year high.
China has traditionally been a big corn exporter. But as Chinese people consume more of their own crop,
exports dropped to 4.5 million metric tons over the first nine months of last year, from 7.1 million tons in 2005.
That number is expected to drop again this year.
India, like other developing nations, has suffered cooking-oil shortages as the budding biofuels industry uses
more palm oil. India cut its import tariff on palm oil five times last year, bringing the levy down to 45% from
88%. "It was the only way they could make cooking oil affordable," says David Laborde, an analyst at the
Washington-based International Food Policy Research Institute. India last year also banned milk-powder
exports.
Food prices aren't just a problem in Asia. The United Nations' global food-inflation index found that food
prices rose 40% last year. A host of countries, including traditional food-export powerhouses Russia,
Argentina and Kazakhstan, have moved to restrict their exports by setting new export tariffs on cereals.
The WTO has no rule against export tariffs. Although the tariffs are opposed by the U.S. and EU, poorer
countries depend on them as a revenue source, taxing commodities such as gold, oil and diamonds.

--- Page 182 ---
Page 182 of 212 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Meanwhile, Western countries are dropping import barriers. A bad harvest in France and the demand for
biofuels meant Europe was short of cereal crops last year. In the first nine months of the year, it imported 2.8
million metric tons of corn from Brazil, up from 117,000 over the same time period in 2005. To increase yield,
the EU also scrapped a rule requiring farmers to set aside 10% of their land to lie fallow.
That wasn't enough. Last month, the EU took the unprecedented step of eliminating tariffs on all cereal
imports. Officially, the tariff suspension is temporary, but EU officials say they expect it to become permanent.
Prices, they believe, will remain high. "We've never been so dependent on imports," says an EU official.
The EU's open market is expected to draw a huge inflow of corn this year, to nine million tons from three
million tons last year, and sorghum, to four million tons from 400,000 tons last year.
"It's a new world, and we need free trade," says Jurjen Ronner, a buyer for De Heus Beheer, a Dutch
company that is one of the world's biggest animal-feed producers. Mr. Ronner is purchasing Thai tapioca and
U.S. sorghum to replace a decline in domestic wheat production.
Other countries, including Brazil, South Korea, Nigeria and Russia, are also cutting import tariffs. The U.S.
Agriculture Department expects South Korea to buy an additional three million tons of corn from the U.S. in
2008.
Nigeria reduced its import tariff on rice last year to 2.7% from 100%. "In some countries, the government is
acting simply to feed people adequately," says Sorin Vasloban, a broker at Paris-based cereal trader
Plantureux SAS.
The U.S., the world's biggest producer, has no import tariffs on cereals, although it does have a levy on most
ethanol imports, which it has no plans to remove.
Economists say reducing import tariffs is a better incentive for producers than erecting barriers to exports.
"Taxing exports means there's less incentive for farmers to produce more, which is what the world needs right
now," says Mr. Laborde of the food-policy institute.
Manipulating tariffs to meet market demands isn't new. In the early 1970s, the Soviet Union suffered from
shortages of soybeans, wheat and other crops. Western powers set stiff export tariffs or quotas to stop
Moscow from buying up too much production. After World War II, weakened European powers protected their
battered farm sector with import tariffs and subsidies.
If food prices fall sharply, the global shift on tariffs could reverse. The U.N. and other agencies forecast that
prices will remain high, although this year better weather is expected to boost production in Europe and
Australia.
"Trade and trade policy are adjusting to a simple fact," says Michael Mann, the EU's agricultural spokesman.
"We used to have too much food, and now we have too little."
---
Lauren Etter in Chicago contributed to this article.
